		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>what a beautifully morbid film this is</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Strangers on a Train shows Alfred Hitchcock at his most shamelessly entertaining in direction, using a script with some razor-wire wit (Raymond Chandler co-wrote it, and one can tell where his style stops and goes), and some really wonderful character actors who are cast so well to type it's almost ridiculous. It's the kind of story that can only happen in movies, especially of the Hitchcock vein; I imagine the 'master' having his kicks making a movie like this, one that doesn't necessarily mess with the audience's head like Psycho, but has that same tension between the good vs. the evil (the latter of which not always as it might seem, to put it mildly). Two well-to-do men meet on a train, one more eager to talk than the other (this one being Robert Walker as Bruno, the elegant, pushy, scathingly funny antagonist to Farley Granger's Haines), and a 'criss-cross' murder scheme is presented forth. This leads the story into a carnival sequence that ranks among Hitchcock's finest (it's also fast-paced too, in a way, it keeps moving- finger snaps- and then leads up to something we know was going to happen, but like 'this' is the surprise).Then, of course, the even more morbid, creatively odd pieces of storytelling make their way into the fabric. It's not a whodunit, but rather an almost satire of the boredom and terrifying double-edged sword nature of the high-life. Bruno may be a truly wicked soul, but he's not totally un-likable; there is something about the Hitchcock villains, like in most of the great stories out there, that make them even more interesting than the protagonist. Not that Granger's performance is bad; he actually has a good handle on the sort of petrified look throughout the film. But it's Walker, and supporting players like Kasey Rogers as Miriam Haines, and surprisingly (maybe not too surprisingly) Hitchcock's daughter Patricia as the young sister with a 'similar' pair of glasses, who pull out the best acting.There are maybe a couple of minor liabilities, if not necessarily in my book then for others; a couple of scenes are so shameless to entertain that the art-value of the film *almost* becomes a little too incredible for its own good. That it's also not scored by the great Bernard Herrmann is also a downer. But other than that, the film is totally unpredictable, and therefore not only gives opportunities for some jaw-drops with its story turns, but also provides some very funny parts, once the tone is set. I sense a little in relation to a Bunuel film at times (Hitchcock did mention Bunuel as one of his favorites), like when Bruno 'crashes' a black-tie party of Haines', and gives two little old ladies an experiment in his ideals. This all leads up to another carnival sequence. I won't say too much of it, aside from it bringing some genuine thrills, but what shocked me was really how it culminated, that when things came to a head, it wasn't at the expense of the total disbelief of the situation. And that song can stick with you for days. This is one of Hitchcok's most entertaining films, still with some of that classy style from his films of the 40's, but also as a kind of bridge to the more outrageous films of his later years.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>camera angle</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It all begins when tennis star Guy Haines (Farley Granger) and Bruno Anthony (Robert Walker) meet on a train. Bruno suggests that they perform tit-for-tat murders, but Guy thinks that it's a joke. Unfortunately, it turns out not to be. And after Bruno carries out his part of the "deal", he expects Guy to do likewise."Strangers on a Train" makes interesting use of camera angle. The opening scene shows cars arriving from opposite sides of the screen, and then the two men get out of the cars and start walking in different directions, implying that they're not the same sort of people. After Bruno murders Guy's wife, he calls to Guy from behind a gate casting a shadow on him; it looks as if he's in jail. When Guy walks over to where Bruno is, it is as if he has entered the same realm as the murderer. As for the possibility of whether or not Bruno was gay, that is a possibility; I read something about the "murderous gays" in Alfred Hitchcock's movies (they may or may not have been gay). But the point is, Hitchcock created another addition to his pantheon of masterpieces.Oh, and I guess that this isn't really related, but does it sway our opinion of the movie knowing Marion Lorne (aka "Bewitched"'s Aunt Clara) played Bruno's mother?</fullReview>

		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Hitchcock Close to His Best (Possible Spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Possible spoilers The title of this film reflects the fact that its plot is set in motion by a chance meeting on a train between two young men, Guy Haines, a leading amateur tennis player, and Bruno Anthony, the son of a wealthy family. Bruno recognises Guy and strikes up a conversation with him. Both men have had difficulties in their private lives. Bruno knows from the gossip columns that Guy is estranged from his unfaithful wife Miriam and is hoping for a divorce to enable him to marry his new girlfriend Anne, the daughter of an influential senator. Bruno himself hates his domineering father who is keeping him short of money. Bruno therefore makes a shocking proposal to Guy. Each of them has motive for murdering a person they hate. They should therefore 'swap murders', so that Bruno kills Guy's wife and Guy kills Bruno's father. The advantage of this is that there would be no motive to connect them to the crimes they actually committed, and each would have an alibi to clear them of the crimes they might be suspected of committing.Guy does not take Bruno seriously, and laughs off his proposal. Bruno, however, who is mentally disturbed, meant what he said in all seriousness, and convinces himself that Guy has agreed to his scheme. He tracks Miriam down, follows her to a funfair and strangles her. Guy, who cannot establish an alibi, becomes the obvious suspect, and the police place him under observation. To make matters worse, Bruno reappears and insists that Guy should keep his side of the 'bargain' by killing his father.The film has (in addition to a number of minor ones) one major plot hole. It is never convincingly explained why Guy does not tell the police about his meeting with Bruno or about Bruno's subsequent admission to Miriam's murder. If, however, one can overlook this point, 'Strangers on a Train' is highly effective both as a thriller and as a character study. Alfred Hitchcock was fascinated by psychology, especially the psychology of evil. Like Norman Bates in 'Psycho', Bruno is a psychological case-study. Spoilt by his doting mother and ignored by his authoritarian father, he has grown up an obsessive loner. He has been obsessed with Guy long before their fateful meeting, and knows all the details not only of his sporting career, but also of his private life. The 'celebrity stalker' is clearly not an exclusively modern phenomenon. Robert Walker's portrayal is a brilliant one, making Bruno both flamboyant and creepy. (This was Walker's last film before his early death). There are hints, which in the moral climate of the early fifties could not be fully developed, that the camp, insinuating Bruno may have homosexual tendencies and that his interest in Guy may derive from sexual motives.The theme of a man wrongly suspected or accused of a crime is a frequent one in Hitchcock's work, from 'The Thirty-Nine Steps' to 'Frenzy'. It is interesting that the hero of this film has a Christian name that is a slang term for 'man'; the idea was possibly to make him an Everyman figure. Farley Granger's Guy is less flamboyant than Walker's Bruno, but no less critical to the success of the film. On the surface he is a handsome, successful and confident athlete, but there are suggestions that underneath he is weak and indecisive. His indecision may stem from feelings of guilt about the failure of his marriage and about Miriam's death- shortly before she died they had a very public quarrel in which he shouted that he could strangle her.The relationship between the two men is very much at the centre of the film- unlike many of Hitchcock's works there is no trademark blonde heroine. (In films dominated by the male characters, Hitchcock often preferred to cast a brunette in the main female role, as here with Ruth Roman). Nevertheless, there are some good performances in minor roles. The best of these is from Laura Elliott as Miriam. In some ways she is a despicable and spiteful character, but in others she is a pitiable one, a rather plain woman who is unable to cope with marriage to a good-looking and successful celebrity and whose promiscuous behaviour results from a need to prove to herself that she can be attractive to other men. As often with Hitchcock, there is comic relief even in the context of a serious film, here provided by the drunken college professor who fails to provide Guy with an alibi and by Marion Lorne as Bruno's dotty mother. Anne's sister Barbara (played by Hitchcock's daughter Patricia) starts off as a comic character (the obnoxiously knowing youngster), but later plays an important and serious part in the film. There are also a number of individual scenes which show the director's touch- the murder itself (seen reflected in Miriam's glasses which have fallen to the ground), the scene where Bruno pretends to strangle an old lady, the scene at the tennis match where all the faces follow the play except Bruno's whose gaze remains fixed on Guy and the scenes set at the funfair, where innocent amusements such as a merry-go-round and a tunnel of love take on sinister aspects. Despite weaknesses in its plot, 'Strangers on a Train' is a good example of Hitchcock working at, or close to, his best. 8/10PS. Despite the coincidence of surnames, I am no relation to the director.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Superb suspense thriller</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The tension and suspense in "Strangers on a Train" do not let up until the end. The screenplay and dialogue are top notch. The cast is not starstudded but it is effective. Not only was it exciting and suspenseful to watch the apparatus spin swiftly out of control with our protagonist and antagonist fighting on it (and various other riders hanging on for their lives who were in the wrong place at the wrong time) but watching the old man climb under the speeding ride to try to stop it (a stunt that was really done without trick photography) was beyond gripping. When the ride is finally stopped, it screeches to such a spectacular halt that although I was watching by myself in my living room, I had to say, `WOW!' out loud. While the story is a classic blackmail suspense tale gone wrong, (Obviously the Coen Brothers took notice) Strangers on a Train is refreshing because of its sheer brilliance. A must see for everyone.--Shelly</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Utterly Satisfying 52 years later</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Nevermind the wags who point out that Farley Granger's performance is strangely uneven. Ignore that all Granger needed to do to prevent murder was to throw his tennis match rather than desperately trying to win it fast. Whatever faults exist, there are so many virtues drowning them out that this is arguably among the top five Hitchcock movies of all time, which means it must be a timeless classic. In fact, it is. Brimming with stylish jolts, dizzyingly inventive cinematography and an unforgettably psychotic performance by Robert Walker, STRANGERS ON A TRAIN is also highly suspenseful, humorous in just the right spots and generally wicked good fun throughout. While Hitch flicks are always interesting and almost always clever, not all of his output is entertaining and some of it has dated. Despite the concept having been recycled by lesser entries like THROW MOMMA FROM THE TRAIN, this particular piece of celluloid is remarkably fresh and innovative. Clearly the master of suspense was beginning to reach the peak of his creative powers, with all-time greats REAR WINDOW, VERTIGO, NORTH BY NORTHWEST and PSYCHO coming in short order thereafter. If you've never seen this and you're a fan of Hitchcock or suspensers in general, I envy you the cinematic experience you're about to partake in. 10/10</fullReview>

		<date>2001-08-30</date>
		<username>Michael DeZubiria (wppispam2013@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>How to make a murder.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Strangers On A Train is one of Alfred Hitchcock's more complex and thorough films. It is a simple but effectively presented story of two strangers who meet on a train ride and end up in a twisted plot of murder and blackmail because of a supposed miscommunication. The film embodies many traditional characteristics of Hitchcock's films, such as the wrong man theme, the steady rise of tension, and even the unenviable portrayal of the police. The inner workings of the criminal justice system as well as the wrong man theme work together in Strangers On A Train to create tension and drive much of the plot, as Guy Haines is forced to prove his innocence on his own because the police have wrongly marked him as the perpetrator of his wife's murder.The characterization in Strangers On A Train is one of the most thorough and interesting parts of the film, particularly in the character of Bruno. When we first meet him, he is simply a passenger on a train who, being a tennis fan, is interested in meeting Guy Haines, who he recognizes as a tennis player. Before long, the conversation turns to Bruno's thoughts about the perfect murder, which casts subtle but effective doubt on his personality, because we still don't know if he is serious about murder or if he is just trying to make conversation. His intentions are gradually revealed as he presses the subject even though Guy shows no serious interest at all, and those intentions are even more subtly but effectively illustrated later in the film.(spoilers) In the scene where Guy calls Anne Morton after an unsuccessful attempt to get his wife to sign divorce documents, Hitchcock uses dialogue, situational irony, sound, and especially editing to foreshadow the murder at the same time as he hints who will perform the murder and even how the murder will be committed. In this scene, Guy is telling Anne that he is so angry with Miriam that he could `just strangle her,' but just as he says this, a train rumbles by and he has to repeat himself. The first thing that this sequence does is re-establish the connection between Guy and Bruno, who met on a train at the beginning of the film. Second, Guy's angry words clearly indicate the kind of frustration that she causes him, providing an example of the type of temper tantrum that inspires all people to `want to kill someone at some point in their lives,' as Bruno later points out to the nice old lady that he almost strangles to death. Third, and probably most important, this scene foreshadows the murder. Not only does Guy unknowingly express a desire to kill his wife in exactly the same way that Bruno soon does, but the scene closes with a dissolve from a frustrated Guy standing in the phone booth talking about strangling his wife to a close up of Bruno's hands, held in a position where they appear to be clenched around an imaginary throat. By this point in the film we know that Bruno is a questionable character at best, and his skewed mentality is illustrated in unusual ways later in the film, many of them during the scene where he is explaining to the elderly women at the party how to commit a perfect murder. During this scene, it is the bizarre and almost disturbing use of sound that is most effective in presenting Bruno's mental state. As he is demonstrating the effectiveness of strangulation on one of the women, he goes into a trance while staring at Barbara (who clearly resembles Miriam and, interestingly enough, is played by Hitchcock's daughter), but it is the fact that circus music is playing during all of this that is the most important. Because it is so out of place at this party, the circus music seems to be playing inside Bruno's head, illustrating his mental instability while also serving to represent the carousel wheel at the circus, which symbolizes the structure of the film's plot. The entire plot of Strangers On A Train is a sort of wheel, or a series of wheels. First of all, it begins with Guy Haines meeting a stranger on a train, and it ends with Guy Haines meeting a different stranger on a train, who he avoids, having recently developed an entirely new outlook about strangers on trains. Also, and more importantly, it involves a circular pursuit. The police are investigating Guy for Miriam's murder, Guy is after Bruno in an effort to get his lighter back and prove his innocence, and Bruno is attempting to plant Guy's lighter at the scene of the murder, thereby leading the police to him and framing him for the crime. The carnival, particularly the carousel, seems to be a way to call attention to the fact that these people are chasing each other around in circles, which is best demonstrated in the high paced finale on the carousel at the end of the film. In what is probably the single most obvious portrayal of incompetent police in this film, an officer literally takes a potshot as Guy and Bruno rush onto the crowded carousel, and the conductor is killed, sending the carousel spinning out of control. The way that the carousel moves at such a high rate of speed and is destroyed at the end represents the frantic speed and destructive power of the wheel-like structure of the murder plot communicated in the story. Hitchcock makes extensive use of cinematic elements like sound and dramatic and situational irony to create suspense and to drive and add depth to the plot of Strangers On A Train, which is one of his more complex films. He uses these things in ways that have a direct impact on the plot and the story and serve to deliver the meaning and increase the substance of the film. Hitchcock uses his intricate knowledge of cinematic elements, as well as excellent acting and skillful direction on his own part, to mold Strangers On A Train into a very intense and enjoyable thriller.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>"Bruno Sometimes Goes a Little Too Far"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While traveling on a train from Washington, D.C. to Long Island, tennis star Guy Haines (Farley Granger) meets Bruno Antony(Robert Walker). They chat amicably and near the end of their journey, Bruno--who knows a great deal about Guy's personal life--makes a bizarre suggestion. He and Guy will exchange murders. He'll murder Guy's clinging wife Miriam, enabling Guy to marry Anne Morton, a senator's daughter; Guy, in turn, will murder Bruno's strict father. Bruno is positive the murders will succeed. If Guy had only known what Bruno's mother told Anne--that "Bruno sometimes goes a little too far"--he would have taken Bruno's plan seriously, instead of dismissing him as a crackpot. Soon Guy learns that Bruno was, dead serious and has kept his end of the bargain: Miriam is found strangled to death in an amusement park. Guy refuses to murder Bruno's father. Incensed, Bruno intends to frame him for Miriam's murder by planting Guy's cigarette lighter at the crime scene. Realizing this, Guy races against time: He must win his tennis match, evade the police who are watching him, and get to the amusement park before Bruno. The showdown between them, a fight on a merry-go-round whirling out of control at tremendous speed, is one of the film's key moments. Casting bay-faced Robert Walker, who had made his mark in boy-next-door roles, as the twisted Bruno was inspired. His performance was excellent. Stealing every scene he's in, Walker's Bruno is certainly one of Hitchcock's most unforgettable villains. Strangers on a Train is one of Hitchcock's best films.</fullReview>

All budding directors should take note! Carousel horses never seemed so menacing! Hitch builds the gripping life-and-death struggle between Bruno and Guy with fabulous edits and remarkably creative camera angles, few other directors could match.What stays with me, though, is the screenplay's close examination of the mind of a psychopath, the terrible ways such a person can affect others, and the warped perspective a psychopath has of the world... Chilling.If you never see another Hitch Movie, consider this one! It's a gem.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Tunnel of love</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Having just reread Patricia Highsmith's excellent suspenseful novel, we looked forward to watching this film again. Not having seen it in a while, the book still in our mind, we couldn't help noticing how the action had been changed around. We still feel that in the context of the excellent screenplay by Raymond Chandler and Czenzi Ormonde, based on the adaptation of the book by Whitfield Cook. The text was turned with an eye to cinematic terms, not to be confused with the different story line of the novel. Supposedly there were two versions, the one for American audiences, and the other for European consumption. We believe the one presented on cable recently is the one used by Hitchcock for domestic viewing.As this tale begins, we watch men's legs and feet moving into the train that is leaving Washington's Union Station for a trip to New York. At the bar car we finally see those legs belong to two passengers, that will be at the center of everything that follows. Bruno Anthony talks to Guy Haines who is seated across from him. Bruno appears to know a lot about Guy, a tennis player of some notoriety. Guy is not comfortable with the man he finds annoying, at best. Bruno, who has steered the conversation into what is the perfect crime, has a strange proposal for Guy. How about exchanging crimes? He would take care of Guy's wife Miriam, who doesn't want to give him a divorce, and in exchange, Bruno expects his newly made friend to do away with his tyrannical father.Guy is horrified at the indecent proposal he had just heard. He doesn't realize that Bruno is a disturbed young man who will stop at nothing to get what he wants. Guy, gets off at Metcalf to have a talk with Miriam, his estranged wife. Being in love with Anne Morton, he wants to have a clean break from the mousy Miriam, who is keeping him from a possible happiness with the woman he loves. He had gone over to the music store where she works where they are seen having a confrontation, in one of the listening booths, as many of the customers watch in horror to their fight. She is adamant in not letting him divorce her.Bruno, who has plans of his own, decides to take a look at the situation first hand. He arrives at Metcalf, a small town, to locate Miriam. He does find her, following her and two male friends to the amusement park in the outskirts of town. Miriam, who notices the strange man tailing her, doesn't know what is going on. Little prepares her for what this mysterious man wants to do to her.Guy, who has been seeing the gorgeous Anne Morton, the daughter of a US senator, is distraught after seeing Miriam. He comes to Washington to be with Anne. Nothing prepares Guy for what he is about to learn: Miriam has been strangled under strange circumstances. The young man knows right away that somewhat Bruno was the assassin. Unfortunately, Bruno has plans for Guy: he must keep his side of the bargain by killing his own father, Mr. Anthony."Strangers on a Train" is one of Alfred Hitchcock best films. Together with "Vertigo", "Shadow of a Doubt", and "Notorious", this film is right up there among the best the director left as his legacy. The novel of Ms. Highsmith is highly charged with homo erotic innuendos. Obviously, Bruno Anthony was gay and saw an opportunity with the weaker Guy Haines to have his way and dominate him, even if the latter one did not want to do his part of the bargain. There are many signs pointing to this conclusion. Mr. Hitchcock plays with our perception of the crime that has been committed. Bruno is a man with a seductive mind who the viewer knows has done a heinous deed, yet we don't cower in fear every time he comes on the scene.The love Guy feels for Anne is affected by the way Bruno is manipulating the situation. Anne realizes that Bruno has an ulterior motive. Even her younger sister, Barbara, knows how to detect a phony whenever she sees one. Bruno misjudges the situation thinking he can blackmail Guy, who has a mind of his own. The ultimate confrontation at the amusement park is one of the best sequences Mr. Hitchcock ever conceived.The casting of this film is what made it work so well. An inspired Robert Walker is the epitome of evil as he proves with his Bruno Anthony. He is seductive and he tries to get Guy, perhaps for a sexual relationship. Farley Granger, who portraits Guy, shows a vulnerable side that Bruno wants to explore. Mr. Granger played in another of Mr. Hitchcock's films "Rope", where he is also seen as a closeted gay man. Although not given the splashiest role, Mr. Granger is believable. Ruth Roman is seen as Anne Morton, a decent woman in love. Patricia Hitchcock, the director's own daughter makes an excellent contribution with her Barbara, the wiser younger sister of Anne. Kasey Rogers plays Miriam and Leo G. Carroll is Senator Morton.Adding to our overall enjoyment of the film is Robert Burks amazing black and white cinematography. Dimitri Tiomkin did an outstanding job with his musical score. Alfred Hitchcock appears briefly in one of his famous cameos almost at the start of the film. The director did a fine job in bringing all the elements into one of his most satisfying pictures that will be loved by his fans.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-09-18</date>
		<username>Cosmoeticadotcom (cosmoetica@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Underrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Strangers On A Train, the 1951 black and white film by Alfred Hitchcock, is a damned good movie- with many of the requisite Hitchcockian flourishes, but it is not a great film, despite many great aspects about it. The reason for this devolves down to one basic fact- it's merely a melodrama, not a true drama. Melodrama always depends upon the propulsion of the plot by the characters within doing the dumbest possible things to get to the next scene. Melodrama thrives on the lowest common denominator. This, of course, does not lessen the enjoyability of the melodrama- be it Hollywood film, soap opera, pro wrestling, etc., but it does remove the work sufficiently from the realistic so that it shows its artifice too much, and therefore never fully involves an engaged viewer.Strangers On A Train includes many recurring Hitchcockian themes- the wrongly accused man, doubles, guilt, assumptions, and, yes, homosexuality. This was a film that came out only a few years after Hitchcock's homosexually themed Rope, in 1948, and even has one of that film's stars- Farley Granger, in this film's lead role. Again, he is a weakling character controlled by a cunning, psychopathic character with definite homosexual leanings; only this time Granger does not play a homosexual character. The film was based upon the first published novel of Patricia Highsmith, whose later Ripley novels earned her the nickname Mistress Of Suspense, in counterpoint to Hitchcock's moniker as Master Of Suspense. The film was adapted for Hitchcock by Whitfield Cook, then handed over to crime novelist Raymond Chandler, who wanted to change many elements. Hitchcock resisted, then gave the final job of adding dialogue to Czenzi Ormonde, and an uncredited Ben Hecht. It's a good screenplay, but, as with many Hitchcock films, it's almost all surface. The depth in most Hitchcock films comes from the actors and their subtleties, not the written words. In this sense Hitchcock's reputation as the ultimate puppetmaster is well earned. Depth was the reason the film was made and actors hired, or else Hitchcock would likely have been satisfied with his storyboards published as early graphic novels?. I suspect that the reason that this film has been ignored, in favor of lesser Hitchcockian fare like Suspicion, To Catch A Thief, or The Man Who Knew Too Much, is because none of the stars in it were superstars. The above mentioned were all good solid works, but they lacked the depth this film does, on however a superficially 'deep' level, in contrast to similar films coming out from Europe at the same time. But, it certainly is a very good film, by any measure, with some deeper subtexts than the script alone entails. If only Hitchcock had been more at home in probing deeper into motivations, and grounding some of his films in reality more, he could have moved past being a mere technical genius, and graced the realm of high art more often than he did, that realm where the European directors did not fear to tread, where high art and mass appeal were not seen as mutually opposing forces. Strangers On A Train is almost a definitive, or archetypal, Hitchcock film in that it has all his film canon's glories and flaws. It is not film noir, nor is it really a thriller, as commonly thought of. We know what is going on at all times, so the suspense is not a whodunit? but a willthevillainsucceed? And it is a darkly comic one at that. Humor is used to gloss over many failures in life, and this film proves that statement true. When you watch Strangers On A Train you will wince, chuckle, smile, shake your head, be frustrated and relaxed, and if that is not the sign of a work of art that does more right than wrong, there are always new Hollywood releases to watch and wince to. And that's a fact not to smile nor chuckle over.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-01-15</date>
		<username>theowinthrop</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Dangers of Celebrity From Psychopaths</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When Alfred Hitchcock made STRANGERS ON A TRAIN it was a harbinger for the bulk of the film going public that he was back at the top of his form. From 1946 to 1950 his films were not box office successes, and his contractual arrangement with David O. Selznick ended because of this. Actually he made some really good films in the period, such as NOTORIOUS, SPELLBOUND, UNDER CAPRICORN, ROPE, and (my opinion) STAGESTRUCK. But while NOTORIOUS AND SPELLBOUND were liked, the audiences were turned off by Hitch's attempts at experimentation: his use of a dream sequence by Salvator Dali in SPELLBOUND was acceptable, as was the setting among psychiatrists. But the experiment with long takes in ROPE confused the public (making ignore the merits of that film), and the willingness of Hitch to have the audience fooled by a lie told by a leading character in a flashback was disliked - far more disliked then it deserved to be - in STAGESTRUCK. A matter of bad timing for UNDER CAPRICORN (it was released just as the scandal of it's star Ingrid Bergman and Roberto Rosallini broke out) made it seem to be a failure (which it wasn't). THE PARADINE CASE was another failure - and one that really had little in it's favor.With STRANGERS ON A TRAIN, Hitchcock shied away from the special tricks that interested him in the recent films. Instead he concentrated on his favorite themes of shared guilt and mistaken identity. Based on a novel by Patricia Highsmith, the story was of how Guy Haines, a professional tennis player, meets a fan of his - Bruno Anthony, a playboy - who is traveling on a cross-country train at the same time as the athlete. Bruno is talkative and fawning, and Guy views him as one of the typical fans he has met...if a little eccentric, with his talk of wanting to be on the first rocket to outer space. But Bruno knows all about Guy's personal problems. He is married to a promiscuous young woman named Miriam, and is in love with Anne Morton, daughter of a Senator. Bruno knows that Miriam won't divorce her successful celebrity husband. Bruno mentions how he can sympathize - he hates his stepfather, who is constantly criticizing him. Then Bruno mentions (sort of off the cuff) an idea he has about two men - technically strangers - exchanging murders for each other and then committing two perfect, unsolvable crimes instead of fumbling them. Guy listens to the idea - and agrees it is an interesting idea. The train reaches Guy's destination, and his last comment is like a validation of the idea. He just does not realize that the "eccentric" Bruno is a sociopath, and believes Guy has just agreed to the two killing each other's foe!It's a wonderfully simple plot actually. Bruno, of course does kill Miriam, but he fully expects Guy to live up to the murder agreement and kill his stepfather. Guy is horrified - and worse, he finds that he is the police department's number one suspect. When Bruno, to encourage Guy, mentions he has the latter's lighter (he pocketed it on the train) and can plant it at the murder site, Guy finds himself in deeper problems - how to avoid the police, and how to control (if possible) the insane Bruno?Hitch always planned the shots of his film carefully, so that from the start we see both our "heroes" from their feet heading towards their fateful meeting in the train with each other. There are constant cross-contacts used in the film to show how the two men are drawn into each other's orbits. It is like fate drawing them together. Yet both have their own personality - and Hitch, with a typical twist, makes the mad Bruno actually more of an attractive figure. His scheme may be vicious (it will kill two people if successful, and he will have Guy live up to the agreement no matter what) but he has a zest for life. One suspects that once Guy too had one, but his marriage's failure, coupled with his now dating a socially prominent woman, has made him more circumspect and dull than he was.The cast is good too. Robert Walker probably had his finest role in this film (only a year before his death). It was a far cry from the homespun boy-next-door of SINCE YOU WENT AWAY or THE CLOCK. His lithe figure also looked quite elegant in tales in several scenes. As Guy Farley Granger acts like a cousin of his co-murderer in ROPE, especially as the circumstances make him increasingly suspicious to the authorities (as his earlier role made his jittery behavior increasingly suspicion to Jimmy Stewart). As for the ladies, Kasie Rogers is properly sluttish as Miriam - enabling the audience to be prepared for her demise (in a famous sequence shot in the reflection of her eyeglasses) so that they actually are cheering Bruno in his act of evil. Patricia Hitchcock appears as the younger sister of Anne (Ruth Roman), who bears a close resemblance to Miriam, and accidentally sets off Bruno at a social occasion. She plays this rare role well. Ruth Roman is properly supportive of Granger, but her role is limiting because she is establishment in her background, and somewhat low keyed. Other supporting performers, Leo G. Caroll as the Senator, Marion Lorne (usually a comedian) as Bruno's dotty mother, and Norma Varden as an unexpected recipient of violence from Walker are all shown to best advantage - all at the hand of a master.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>He ain't called "The Master of Suspense" for nothing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alfred Hitchcock is widely known as "The Master of Suspense" and with good reason. Nobody could fashion such white-knuckle tension through the mere power of suggestion as he could. Viewed today, anyone of his masterpieces is just as thrilling and quickly paced as when they originally were released. A perfect example of his brilliance would be "Strangers on a Train". The concept is memorable, but many of the events seem a bit far fetched and even ridiculous on closer inspection. However, Hitchcock's direction is so masterful he makes even seemingly implausible situations brimming with suspense. "Strangers on a Train" remains one of his most suspenseful films. Its one of Hitchcock's most interesting variants on the "Wrong Man" theme.The performances are a bit uneven. As usual, Farley Granger is rather wooden. Just as in "Rope", hes completely overshadowed by his costar. Robert Walker gives easily his best performance as the psychotic Bruno Anthony. Hitchcock knows how to manipulate the audience into liking his character. Hes witty, refined, and unafraid to speak his mind. Hes a much more compelling protagonist than Farley Granger's. Add to this several delightful supporting performances (Patricia Hitchcock, Leo G. Carroll, Marion Lorne) and another great score from Dimitri Tiomkin and you have film of perfect entertainment value. (9/10)</fullReview>

		<username>José Luis Rivera Mendoza (jluis1984)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Two strangers, one immortal masterpiece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Patricia Highsmith's gripping tale of the meeting of two strangers with nothing in common except the desire of murder, was the origin of one of the Master of Suspense's most praised and successful movies. Overshadowed by the rest of his 50s movies, "Strangers on a Train" is one of Alfred Hitchcock's best films, and definitely a landmark not only in his career, but also in history of film.During a small trip on a train from Washington to New York, Bruno Anthony (Roger Walker) meets tennis professional Guy Haines (Farley Granger) and soon a friendly bond is set between them. During lunch, they discover that they have something in common: both have someone in their lives they would like to kill. Bruno comes with a bizarre intriguing solution proposing that each should kill the other's "problem". Guy takes the conversation as a joke, but soon he'll discover that Bruno was really serious about it, dead serious about it.Hitchcock's takes us to a haunting dark world in this movie as Bruno reveals as one of the scariest sociopaths on screen. Robert Walker literally steals every scene he is in and domains the screen with a powerful and unforgettable performance as Bruno Anthony. Sadly, it was his last performance but what a performance is this. Farley Granger is also very good as Guy, although Walker totally steals the show.The complex relation between Guy and Bruno is something that Hitchcock explores with brilliant care (considering the strict rules of the time). Bruno is the Mephistopheles who just granted Guy/Faust's most desired wish and is waiting for his partner to pay the debt. He is the tempter who brings Guy to the dark side, while Guy tries to escape his pact even when he really enjoys the results of it.Guilt and fear corrode him and Farley Granger makes the best of the character. The sexual tension between them is also something that, like Highsmith in the novel, Hitchcock uses to increase the suspense and tension in the audience. While in the American version of the film this is not as apparent, the British cut implies it subtly and it is possibly the best version of the film.Hitchcock cleverly creates haunting imagery using Washington's landscape as stage for his noir-inspired tale. His style was totally developed at that time and his use of light and shadows is simply brilliant. The beautiful camera-work and the very creative set-pieces (the tennis match scene is simply perfect) complete this film that is nothing short of perfect.This often forgotten masterpiece marks the beginning of Hitchcock's supreme reign over American cinema and, like his previous work "Stage Fright", continues the walk through the dark side that would lead to "Psycho"'s Norman Bates and "Vertigo"'s Scottie Ferguson. It is definitely an important film in the Master's career and one that everyone interested in cinema must watch at least once. 10/10</fullReview>

		<date>1999-12-13</date>
		<username>Michael Coy (michael.coy@virgin.net)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"It's Too Fantastic!"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It was tempting to put as the one-line summary of this film "Farley Granger's On A Train", or even "Bruno Picks Up Guy En Route To Metcalf", but I resisted. Oddly enough, this straight-faced murder thriller would be well-served by frivolous captions, having itself a strangely flippant feel.Two men meet by chance on a train journey between Washington and New York. One is a clean-cut sports hero, the other a languid rich boy. Each has an inconvenient family-member - in the case of Guy the tennis star, it is his unfaithful wife, who stands between him and Anne, the woman he loves, and in Bruno's case it is his overbearing father. Bruno proposes that they swap murders - each should kill the other's problem relative. This will make the murders undetectable, because in each case there will be nothing to link the killer to the crime.We meet both men by means of their feet - which is how they encounter each other. The opening sequence has each man making his way to the train, filmed from the knees down. Guy is sober and conservative, and the tennis racquets dangled by the porter tell us that he is an athlete. Bruno's flashy shoes give him away - he is a mincing mommy's boy. As they sit opposite one another in the train's observation car, their feet brush ... and Bruno has created his opportunity.The relationship between the two men has a definite homosexual flavour to it. Their brief encounter smacks of a gay pick-up in an age when such things had to be done surreptitiously, and Guy's gradual relenting - agreeing first to a drink, then a meal - allows the flirtatious Bruno to take liberties. Robert Walker is wonderful as the unhinged Bruno, by turns beguiling and sinister. Farley Granger makes a good Guy Haines, the handsome tennis star whose girlfriend (Anne Morton, played by the lovely Ruth Roman) is smarter than he is.Raymond Chandler co-wrote the screenplay, and it shows. The Guy-Bruno pairing of opposites is imitated in the Hennessy-Hammond twosome, the cops yoked together by their work. Of course, neither Bruno nor Guy has to bother with work, the one being idly rich, the other being groomed for a career in federal politics. The merry-go-round stands as a double metaphor - firstly a symbol of these characters' frothy lives, the pleasant treadmill which they cannot quit, and latterly (when it gets out of control) a symbol of Bruno's psychosis, attractive in the early stages but terrifying when it breaks free of its constraints.The first fairground scene is vintage Hitchcock. Miriam loses our sympathy from the outset, cavorting with two toy boys. This is necessary, lest we blame Guy for her subsequent fate. Bruno's brooding presence is the very distillation of evil. His meanness to the little boy with the balloon strips away the veneer of his charm. We see Bruno unbuttoned for the first time. Miriam's awareness of Bruno tautens our nerves almost unbearably - she vacillates between sexual fear and sexual fascination, a duality which is reflected in the jollity of the setting and the ugliness of what we know will happen.Bruno stalks both Guy and the film itself like an evil angel. We see him black against the floodlit monument, a troubling speck against the imposing regularity of the columns. In the crowd at the tennis match, Bruno alone fails to follow the flight of the ball, side to side. His head remains cruelly still, staring at Guy. He brushes aside his final opportunity for repentance, sticking to his malicious campaign to damage Guy long after it can achieve anything, in a nihilistic negation of life itself.Some of the film's propositions don't hold water. Primarily, it is too much to believe that an aspiring politician and public hero like Guy would be duped into supposing that he can't go to the cops over the behaviour of this crank. In the atmospheric scene at the wrought-iron gate, Guy slips over to Bruno's side of the gate when the police car arrives, symbolising his 'crossing-over'. In real life, such a man would be too level-headed, and would have too much to lose, to be swayed by Bruno. Leo G. Carroll is far too English to be acceptable as a Washington senator, and police officers investigating murders don't confront suspects with their own alibi wirnesses. The notion that Guy would give his police 'minders' the slip and appear to be following Bruno's orders is crazy. If he were caught before exposing Bruno, he would not have a leg to stand on.It is Anne who solves the crime. She spots the elaborate necktie which proves that the visitor is no stranger, and she sees the significance of the spectacles. As an interesting footnote, Anne's kid sister Barbara, the girl with the big personality, was played by Hitchcock's daughter Patricia.The film has some characteristically flamboyant Hitchcock moments. The scene in the study is shot from a bird's eye view, both to evoke a sense of unease and to emphasise Guy's suppressed feelings of guilt - we see what Hennessy cannot, the concealed gun in the bureau drawer. The final climax is pure Hitchcock, with Guy needing to beat Bruno to the island, but first having to get through a five-set tennis match.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>This time I don't buy it</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Two men (strangers) meet (on a train), as per the title. One is a tennis player and one is a pretty creepy guy with "big ideas" (read: the enemy), they hit it off pretty well, but the second man turns out to get too involved with his ideas and ends up killing the tennis player's wife for him after the tennis player nonchalantly mentions he wouldn't mind having her out of the way. Now it's a rush to see what the tennis player will do now that he's a suspect and the other man keeps appearing to urge him to kill the stalker's father, via an agreement he didn't make.So it's basically all of Hitchcock's usual fare... a man on the run, wrongly accused; a man blackmailing him; bumbling cops; a woman who wants to figure it out but doesn't know who to trust initially before fully trusting the protagonist; and old ladies who giggle over discussions of murder.This time I don't buy it, though. The whole lighter thing comes off as a real gimmick to me (even when the stalker guy drops it in the grate... after the initial suspense, it doesn't matter anyway 'cause he's waiting for dusk.) I couldn't believe that there isn't some way the protagonist couldn't have gone to the police and proved that he wasn't guilty, if anything because of the two characters they were. The drunken professor was a useless side-note. This movie is all of Hitchcock's stuff, but none of his experimentation which is really the stuff I like.In all, this is the most suspenseful of his movies, but I can't gather up the energy to care for it. Once again, I have an issue with the characters. I'm just so used to Hitchcock telling me the same story over and over again that frankly I'm beginning to feel like he's repeating himself. The guy had a few recurring themes, sure, but this is starting to fall into merely obsessive pondering of the many different ways to present the same internal fear Hitchcock has of being wrongfully imprisoned. And with each movie of his I see, instead of said fear making me feel like I'm seeing some auteur at work, I'm beginning to see a guy who needs to relax a little. I like his much more experimental stuff, where he plays with camera and imagery and narrative and design. This "suspense thriller" stuff with the typical motifs is getting very dull, which is a terrible thing.--PolarisDiB</fullReview>

		<username>Brian Berta</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Another great Hitchcock film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hitchcock is famous for making crime dramas. Some of his most praised ones include "Rear Window", "Dial M for Murder", "Vertigo", "North by Northwest", and "Rope" (which I will be watching soon). He has great talent in making so many of them succeed in so many different ways. He gives them suspenseful and disturbing feelings which still feel unnerving today. "Strangers on a Train" may not be the very best film he's ever made, but it's still very effective.Guy Haines, a tennis star, meets a fan of his named Bruno Antony while riding on a train. Bruno tells him his theory for how 2 strangers can get away with murder. He says that if 2 unrelated people swap murders and kill the person that the other wants dead, there would be no clues to connect, and both of them will be able to get away. Guy Haines doesn't take him seriously, but Bruno plans to implement his theory.Bruno Antony served as a very memorable villain. Instead of acting hostile or impatient, he would simply stalk Guy everywhere he went, and would continue to try and get him to follow through with his end of the deal. This made for some unnerving scenes which made the viewer wonder what he would do to Guy. Bruno would constantly stalk him in public, call him, and send him supplies necessary for murdering his father. As Bruno started to talk to Guy's family, the movie continued to raise tension as the movie made the viewer wonder whether he was going to do something to his family. Also, Bruno Antony was played exceptionally well by Robert Walker. He commands every scene he's in, and he captures just the right mood for Bruno's character.The conversation that Bruno and Guy have on the subway near the beginning of the film is very thought-provoking. It makes the viewer wonder if such a perfect murder like the one that Hitchcock described actually is possible. It seems to make sense, and if the criminal doing the crime does a few minor things to protect his identity, the crime could be executed flawlessly. The film keeps the viewer believing that this crime would work throughout the film, and he does this so well that the viewer starts to suspect that a happy ending is next to impossible. A great tactic that Hitchcock did in this movie and "Dial M for Murder" is that he created 2 crimes that seemed very believable and creative. It wasn't until the ending where he revealed a clue so minimal that the viewer likely would not have ever seen it coming no matter how much they were paying attention. What makes the film more terrifying is that the crime Bruno thought of could easily happen to any person. The chances of it happening are highly unlikely, but it's the idea that anyone could be a murderer which is central to the movie and that's what makes it work better.Hitchcock is a master at visual set pieces, and he utilized his skill for them here. One of the scenes happens near the beginning where it shows Bruno stalking Miriam, the person who he plans to kill, at a carnival. Since she's with 2 of her boyfriends, the viewer has doubts that Bruno can overpower all of them. However, the film shows both of her boyfriends doing a "High Striker" carnival game. None of them get it to the top. However, Bruno goes up after both of them, and he gets it to the top easily. Later, it shows her and her boyfriends in a boat going through a "Tunnel of Love". Bruno is right behind them. It shows their shadows on a wall with Bruno's shadow slowly moving closer to them. The shot cuts to the opening where it shows Miriam screaming. However, they go out and it shows one of her boyfriends simply messing around with her. Hitchcock tricks the viewer in that scene.Another great visual set piece comes at one of the film's most iconic scenes. Guy is at the tennis game waiting to play next. The camera is pointed at the crowd. Every body is turning their heads to follow the tennis ball. For a second, the viewer might smile a bit. However, the camera zooms in to show Bruno staring directly at Guy, giving the scene a sudden, serious tone. Another scene happens when Guy is quietly wandering through the darkness of Bruno's home. A dog growling at him at the top of the stairs throws the viewer off from what they think might happen, and the scene somewhat surprises the viewer.Bruno's perfect murder theory was well-thought out. However, I never understood why Bruno told Anne about his plan of putting a lighter at the scene of the crime to frame Guy. That was a very foolish decision for him to do. Anne could've easily told her family about what he did, and they could've all attempted to stop him, ruining his plan. This isn't necessarily a gigantic plot hole, but it still bugs me as it effects the entire final act. Also, I felt like the final confrontation between Guy and Bruno being on an out-of-control merry-go-round wasn't exactly the best setting Hitchcock could've picked as it was slightly cheesy for such a serious crime film about murder.In conclusion, this was a very great Hitchcock film. Bruno was a great villain, and great acting plus clever visual set pieces made Bruno pretty memorable. Hitchcock's thought-provoking perfect murder theory was intriguing, and it was very interesting. It may have a few minor flaws, but it has still accomplished a lot, and it was well worth my time.</fullReview>

		<username>ElMaruecan82</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Our mamas always said "never talk with strangers", you never know what you're gonna get...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>All our mothers told us to never talk with strangers. Unfortunately, we often tend to overlook these childhood advice on the simple basis that we're grown-ups, but this is perhaps the most important lesson from one of Alfred Hitchcock's most popular film, "Strangers on a Train".When tennis-player Guy Haines (Farley Granger) is approached by co-traveler Bruno Anthony (Robert Walker), the latter becomes so annoyingly nosy, asking so many indiscreet questions about column gossips and Guy's divorce, and gets so eager to expose his theory of the 'perfect murder' that we can't believe that Guy's 'lunatic'-radar didn't sound alarm bells in his mind. But Guy is a soft one, he lets Bruno talk, too shy or too polite to cut the discussion short, unaware that each hesitation gives Bruno enough territory to conquer. And this is integral to the whole pervert psychological game between the two leads, Guy's weakness allowed Bruno to explicitly describe his idea of the perfect crime: crisscross, much to Guy's disbelief, and to our pervert fascination.The idea is simple: the two men have someone in their lives they would rather dispose of, so basically, they swap the subsequent murders: Guy kills Bruno's father, and Bruno kills Guy's vulgar soon-to-be divorced pregnant wife Miriam (Laura Elliott). That way, each one can get himself a perfect alibi and no one gets ever suspected. Bruno solves his Oedipal issues and Guy can marry the classier (and politically interesting) senator's daughter Anne Morton (Ruth Roman). It is a clever idea, although morally repugnant. However, there's never one word or attitude from Guy to convince Bruno that he's not interested. Guy nodded to Bruno's project, afraid to hurt him, out of shyness and weakness, so Bruno might be an evil lunatic but Guy is guilty out of weakness and spinelessness.There was an interesting quote from Truman Capote in "In Cold Blood" about the two thugs who massacred the Cluster family, they weren't killers and would never have killed anyone alone, but together they formed a sort of third personality that could take the plunge. This duality is omnipresent in "Strangers on a Train" and since Hitch has never been stingy on expressive visuals, doubles are constant leitmotifs in the film starting with the iconic image of the two railroad tracks crossing each other, illustrating Bruno's twisted idea. Characters too are in two copies, two women with glasses (Pat Hitchcock as Anne's kid sister bears a resemblance with the victim), there are two cops, two sets of feet walking, two glasses of whiskey ordered, and ultimately two murderers. Guy isn't a murderer, but after his wife's death, and without any alibi to hang his hope on, he's incapable to accuse Bruno and inevitably acts guilty, this is a side effect of the crisscross. And I think the popularity of "Strangers on a Train" is due to Hitchcock's fondness on concepts: movies that could be summed up in a simple but clever idea, "Rope", "Lifeboat", "Shadow of a Doubt" ? and this one had the meatiest one. Speaking for myself, I was so enthusiastic about the premise that I felt cheated about the story only featuring one, I wanted the crisscross, but then I realized it couldn't work, with two guilty guys, the film could only be as a detective story. In this case, it's a story of guilt, responsibility, harassment and personal torment.Indeed, there is in "Strangers on a Train" the same grain that would make such films as "Fatal Attraction", when an impressionable protagonist gets carried away by a manipulative and dangerous villain. The performances are crucial, Granger is nervous, and tries to slip away from the situations rather than facing them, Bruno is a spoiled mama's boy who believes things should be done his way, yet coming from a wealthy background, he can easily hide his dark nature behind a friendly smile. And this is why he's one of Hitch's best villains, Bruno creates a sort of active/passive interaction that gives a few hints of homoerotic subtext, as if Bruno was a sort of Alpha male who had the guts to follow his wicked instinct while Guy hypocritically condemns the murder, but benefits from its effect while hiding with his future in-laws, still concerned by his political ambitions. And on the basis of this two-character study, the film takes off, driven with a nervous energy and highlighted by a superb black and white cinematography from the murder seen through the victim's eyeglasses' lens to the villain whose head doesn't move while watching the tennis game. In Patricia Highsmith's novel, Guy was an architect, but the choice of a tennis player is more than a colorful addition, it contributes to the thrilling sequence where Guy tries to win the game in three sets so he can confront Bruno in the scene of crime, only to be delayed by his opponent's determination to win the game. Bruno is also delayed by a little incident involving the one piece of evidence of the crime and it's like a symbolic tennis game between the film's players. This sequence is only the first high point before one of the most iconic climactic scenes from a Hitchcock film. Hitch loved to set his climaxes in memorable places, in "Strangers on a Train", it's one of his most spectacular and spellbinding moments, and such moments were necessary because the script isn't devoid of plot contrivances and demand a lot in terms of suspension of disbelief. But Hitchcock cared for plausibility without being enslaved by rational requirements, he wanted to delight the audiences with cinematic tricks and feature psychologically absorbing situation. Hitch could get away with it, because he mastered the technique well-enough to con us and make the unbelievable believable, not because it is, but because we want to, for the sake of enjoyment. And "Strangers on a Train" IS one of his most enjoyable films? and one hell of a noir thriller.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>One of my favorite Hitchcock movies</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is one of my favorite Hitchcock movies ? it has a fantastic premise and cast, and also one of his very best murder scenes (and that's saying something!) Hitchcock uses great economy in the first half of the film; right out of the chute two strangers meet on a train, and one proposes the 'perfect murder', one in which there is no apparent motive because the two simply 'criss cross' murders for each other.Robert Walker is absolutely perfect as the sociopath who proposes this scheme to the straight-laced tennis player, played well by Farley Granger. He wants his overbearing father out of the picture, and knows that Granger wants a divorce from his wife, having done his homework. Granger politely declines, and while his motivation increases when his adulterous wife (Laura Elliot) manipulatively tells him she no longer wants to split from him in the next scene, he still wants no part of murder. However, in the very next scene Walker goes forward with his 'end of the bargain' anyway, stalking Elliot at a carnival in an outstanding sequence. She's aware of him staring at her and even flirts with him a little bit, and as he follows her through the Tunnel of Love out to 'Magic Isle' it's seriously spine-tingling. Hitchcock shows her getting strangled in a reflection from her glasses which have fallen to the ground. These first few scenes, from the train to Magic Isle, are a masterpiece.Granger is of course horrified to hear about this, and while he intends to move on to woman he's already been seeing (Ruth Roman), he doesn't intend on committing a murder he never agreed to. Walker begins stalking him and putting pressure on him, and there are fantastic scenes at the Jefferson Memorial (him staring down a distance and high up on the stairs), as well as at a tennis match (the crowd following a volley, turning their heads back and forth; Walker staring straight ahead at Granger).It is true that the film slows down slightly in the second half, but it's by no means 'slow' ? there are several other great scenes, we feel real tension as Granger finds himself mired in a creepy lunatic's fantasy come to life (channeling Hitchcock's 'wrong man' theme), and it has a thrilling climax, but I won't spoil it any further. I have to say I loved the spunky character played by Patricia Hitchcock, the director's daughter, and it's a shame she didn't get more work as an actor. It's also a shame that Robert Walker died at age 32, shortly after the film's release. He certainly lives on in this role, and this film more than stands the test of time. Excellent.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I have always loved suspenseful movies and I have found that the ones that work best at generating suspense for me involve situations where I can almost see the scenario playing out in real life. It's hard to believe that such a scenario could happen in real life, but I found myself convinced that demons were real. This was all due to the documentary style of the film. On the drive home from the movie theater, I kept the overhead light in my car on and was constantly checking my backseat. Alfred Hitchcock's Strangers On a Train accomplishes this believability, but does so in a much different way. When we first meet Bruno on the train, I was immediately struck by how familiar the character was to me. I know people who behave just like him, minus all of the homicide and craziness. They are outgoing to the point of extreme annoyance. This familiarity made me immediately view the character more three dimensionally.When Bruno finally reveals his plan to Guy, I found myself thinking that it was a relatively good plan. By making both the antagonist and his scheme believable, I was locked in for the rest of the movie. Robert Walker does a tremendous job of playing the complex Bruno. He is anything but a one-note character. While he came across as somewhat silly at times, I found Bruno to be a pretty scary villain due to his unpredictability. Farley Granger's performance wasn't as strong to me, but he managed to hold his ground with Walker. Hitchcock does what he does best in this picture and creates suspense even in scenes where you think everything is fine. The most chilling scene in the entire movie to me occurred in broad daylight. Guy is in a car and looks out the window to see Bruno standing on a flight of stares far away, looking back at him. While this film isn't nearly as suspenseful as Psycho, I enjoyed it thoroughly and found myself on edge numerous times.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Calls for some suspension of disbelief but pretty good</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The movie is based on an interesting concept. The law cannot link a murderer to the murder he has committed if a motive cannot be established. Hence, if two strangers want to do away with someone in their lives, they can each commit the murder for the other. Robert Walker is brilliant as the scheming, conscienceless bad guy. He casts his shadow even on the scenes he is not present in and you never cease to wish him ill. Farley Granger is pretty good as the morally-upright gentleman who falls unwittingly into Walker's trap. His character may appear naive at times but that is the writing. Patricia Hitchcock is charming and brings some much needed cheer to the movie. Ruth Morton, Granger's love interest, is the weak link as she delivers a monotonous performance. Marion Lorne, thought she has only a couple of scenes, leaves a lasting impression as Walker's annoyingly over-indulgent mom.This movie is by no means flawless. In fact it is full of gaping plot holes and we can guess the ending because it was made the days of Hays Code. However, it is fast paced and does not give us time to linger on the low points for long. We are treated to some very good shots. (We see a murder as reflected on the shades fallen in the grass). The tension is heavy and the characters are believable. It will not hold up to a logical scrutiny but it is a good idea if you are looking for some light entertainment.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>nice</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Theatre is theatre, film film. Right? Actually not any more as the two mediums increasingly converge. And the bizarre fact about this production, although based on the 1949 Patricia Highsmith novel rather than the subsequent Hitchcock movie, is that it feels, for much of the evening, like a piece of film noir. This is theatre turning into cinema rather than borrowing from it. The evening begins with the image of an onrushing train. We then shift to a public compartment where Guy Haines has his fateful encounter with Charles Bruno. But everything about the first half suggests we are in for a night at the movies. We get multiple short scenes. Tim Goodchild's busily revolving set is shadowed by Peter Wilms's video projections so that we see the landscape through which the train is passing. To clinch matters, the sound design deliberately echoes the nerve-jangling scores that Bernard Herrmann wrote for Hitchcock. Craig Warner has written the script. It felt sometimes as if it was more the work of Warner Brothers.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Inventive, well-paced Hitchcock story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>STRANGERS ON A TRAIN is an example of Hitchcock at his purest as well as his inventive best: it's a straightforward, suspense-laden thriller with an intriguing premise, some engaging development and a wonderfully executed payoff. Raymond Chandler's work on the screenplay adds a grittily realistic spin on what could have been, in other hands, an overly artificial thriller, but in these hands it's one of the director's best.The story is simplicity in itself: Farley Granger, so good as the killer in ROPE, is another of Hitch's "wronged man" heroes, really put through the wringer by the throes of the screenplay; Robert Walker bags the best role of his career as a pitch-perfect deranged bad guy who might well be the finest of Hitch's villains, at least until Anthony Perkins came along later. I particularly liked the presence of Patricia Hitchcock in a blackly comic supporting role, along with turns for veterans like Leo G. Carroll.The movie slow-builds its storyline at a gradual pace, building tension upon tension, incident upon incident, until finally reaching breaking point in which Hitch directs a tennis match. Imagine what he could have done with Wimbledon! It gets ever better after that, with the fun fair climax which is one of my favourite sequences in the director's filmography, a truly outlandish set-piece which has it all: suspense, death, danger, a hard-bitten fight scene, incompetent policemen and a rousing score to top it all of. It's a great way to end a great little movie.</fullReview>

		<username>Scott44</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Magical</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Strangers on a Train is one of those magical experiences in cinema where every moment works; and only small quibbles can be made even sixty one years after it was released. Visually, it is magnificent. The depth of precision that Hitchcock achieves makes watching it a singularly pleasurable experience. I've seen it now at least ten times; each new time I see it I wish it was the first viewing all over again.Robert Walker is particularly mesmerizing as the (implied) gay psychopath, Bruno Antony. (In real-life he was heterosexual. Farley Granger, in real life a homosexual, plays the straight tennis player Guy Haines who gets drawn into Bruno's murder-swapping scheme.) Patricia Hitchcock is another standout. Her father gave her a delicious role; and she steals scenes with glee.Strangers on a Train is so solid that criticism of it seems unjustified. We certainly don't want to object to one character's bizarre idea late in the film of asking nearby people to assist in recovering the same cigarette lighter he expects will cast irrefutable guilt on the other character. Hitchcock's films all have plot weaknesses (a.k.a., "MacGuffins") built-in and he needs the audience to suspend disbelief.However, I cringe as the tennis scenes unfold. First, the over-the-top announcer speaks as if neither he nor anyone in the audience has ever watched a tennis match before. ("Guy Haines has a two-set lead and if he wins another, it will be a straight-set win for sure." Oh, really? Is that what a three-set match means?) The two players also appear to play a let serve, which is a blooper.There's also Hitchcock's repetition of a player nearly facing us and hitting what appears to be a serve or overhead for a winner. A real elite tennis player wouldn't be hitting that shot with his stomach directly facing the viewer. Also, 1950s tennis didn't have that many service winners--and they wouldn't be hitting so many overheads from such a similar and comfortable position.(BTW, I've read other user reviews and many writers have said that Haines is a tennis pro. Wrong. He's an amateur. There's a subtle distinction which I think is worth bringing up because of how it helps define what Guy Haines is about.) Quibbles aside, I wonder what it would be like if Hitchock had convinced William Holden to play Guy Haines. Granger looks more like a tennis player than Holden, and seems more believably manipulated by Bruno than perhaps Holden would have been. On the other hand, there would have been a more uncertain feeling if Holden as Haines had paid that evening call on a stranger.Strangers on a Train is a perennial pleasure. If it is playing anywhere close by go see it again.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Desertman84</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One Of Hitchcock's Most Underrated Films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Strangers on a Train is an psychological thriller film produced and directed by Alfred Hitchcock.It was based on the novel of the same title by Patricia Highsmith. The film stars Farley Granger, Ruth Roman, and Robert Walker together with Leo G. Carroll, Patricia Hitchcock, and Laura Elliott.This perfectly exemplifies Hitchcock's favorite theme of the evil that lurks just below the surface of everyday life and ordinary men.Tennis pro Guy Haines chances to meet wealthy wastrel Bruno Anthony on a train. Having read all about Guy, Bruno is aware that the tennis player is trapped in an unhappy marriage to to wife Miriam and has been seen in the company of senator's daughter Ann Morton. Baiting Guy, Bruno reveals that he feels trapped by his hated father. As Guy listens with detached amusement, Bruno discusses the theory of "exchange murders." Suppose that Bruno were to murder Guy's wife, and Guy in exchange were to kill Bruno's father? With no known link between the two men, the police would be none the wiser, would they? When he reaches his destination, Guy bids goodbye to Bruno, thinking nothing more of the affable but rather curious young man's homicidal theories. And then, Guy's wife turns up strangled to death. Given a good basis for a thriller in the Patricia Highsmith novel and a first-rate script, Hitchcock embroiders the plot into a gripping, palm- sweating piece of suspense.This intriguing film deals with all the auteur's issues, including the double motif, moral ambiguity, fine line between hero and villain.In addition to that,it is an expertly made thriller with fluid camera work, dark humor and enough cliff-hanging build up to keep you biting your nails until there's nothing left. Easily one of Hitchcock's most underrated films.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"What is a life or two, Guy? Some people are better off dead. Well, like your wife and my father, for instance."It's the perfect murder. Two strangers meet, and each kill off the victim of the other. There's no motive linking the killer to the crime, so theoretically the police would never have a reason to suspect what actually happened. Of course, even the perfect plan can fail when one of the participants has no intention of actually going through with the crime, and the other is mentally disturbed.We've seen Hitchcock tackle "the perfect murder" before in Dial M for Murder, and it's no real surprise that both movies deal with the disastrous results of a crime that turns out to be not nearly so perfect. I also ended up liking both movies to about the same degree.Strangers on a Train does one thing extremely well, and that's suspense. It may be one of the most suspenseful Hitchcock movies that I've seen, and that's saying something. The merry-go-round scene at the end was incredibly impressive, but it probably will be quite a while before I revisit this one. With that said, an average Hitchcock movie is still worth seeing at least once.&nbsp;</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Inspired casting in classic Hitchcock</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Strangers on a Train" features some of the most inspired casting of any Hitchcock movie.Robert Walker was quite close to a nervous breakdown during the making of this film. Reportedly, Hitchcock was aware of the actor's instability and found Walker perfect for the role of the demented Bruno Antony.Completing this odd couple is the good looking, well-mannered Farley Granger, as tennis pro Guy Haines. Talk about opposites attracting, Granger, who was gay in real life, is the antithesis of the Antony character. Soft spoken and a tad light in his loafers, his Guy Haines mistakenly concludes that Bruno is just a neurotic, rich kid. It's too late before he realizes that Bruno is a sociopathic murderer.This is easily one of Hitchcock's finest films. Hitchcock's failed experiment "Rope" tried to bring the Loeb and Leopold story to the screen. But this film succeeds as HItchcock's own Loeb and Leopold story.There are many fine moments in this film. Bruno entering the "Tunnel of Love" on a boat named "Pluto" (god of the underworld). Bruno casually popping a child's balloon with a cigarette. Marion Lorne, playing Bruno's silly, doting mother, showing her son her latest painting, a gruesome abstract which she calls "St. Francis". Bruno starts laughing upon seeing it, mistaking it for a portrait of his father.The murder scene of Miriam is classic Hitchcock. Murder as art would be encapsulated forever in the classic shower scene of "Psycho". This murder in "Strangers on a Train" may be even better, as it is subtly portrayed through the distorted image of the murdered woman's glasses.Walker as Bruno is easily one of Hitchcock's darkest villains. HItchcock had a dark side. How many film directors would take advantage of an actor's mental problems?Hitchcock would and did, and pulled off one of his finest films.</fullReview>

"Strangers on a Train" must be grouped with director Alfred Hitchcock's very best films. And, with so many of his themes represented, it could be - if any one film could be described as such - the perfect Hitchcock picture. Or, at least, it's the first perfect culmination of themes.The director's famed touches are masterfully woven throughout, from the voyeurism of those watching Granger argue with his bespectacled, pregnant wife (by another man, no less) - to the homo-orgasmic final confrontation between passively athletic Granger and charismatically psycho Walker (those steel rods really move). Hitchcock, the perfectly cast male duo, Robert Burks' photography, and motherly Marion Lorne are at "Perfect Ten" performance levels.********** Strangers on a Train (6/30/51) Alfred Hitchcock ~ Farley Granger, Robert Walker, Ruth Roman, Marion Lorne</fullReview>

Hitchcock imbues the entire film with an incredibly ominous, eerie feeling with the help of shots of Bruno in the distance, silently watching Guy, waiting for him to return the favor of murder. To go into any more detail on the story would spoil it, but it's a chilling tale with some fine performances to boot. Another masterwork from the master of suspense.</fullReview>

		<username>ShootingShark</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Suspenseful Hitchcock Thriller From Patricia Highsmith Crime Fiction Classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Guy Haines is a tennis-player whose philandering wife won't grant him a divorce. Bruno Antony is an idle rich psychopath who wants to kill his father. The two meet by chance on a train journey, wherein Antony hatches a mad plan; he will kill Haines' wife and Haines will kill Antony's father. Haines thinks he's a harmless fool and humours him but Antony is deadly serious and kills Mrs Haines. Guy must somehow clear his name with the police and expose Antony as the real killer.Patricia Highsmith's superb first novel is a brilliant idea - two unconnected men swap murders so that neither has a possible motive for their crime - which fits in perfectly with Hitchcock's classic double-hunt structure - the hero must hunt the killer while the authorities hunt him. This is the recipe that makes this movie so enjoyable - watching the cool and sophisticated Granger squirming as he tries desperately to extricate himself from a mess that isn't of his making. The script itself (by Raymond Chandler &amp; Czenzi Ormonde) is workmanlike, but what really interests Hitchcock is racking up the tension in several memorable sequences - the lengthy, wordless stalking in the amusement park leading up to the murder, flashy crosscutting between Granger winning a tennis-match and Walker trying desperately to retrieve a cigarette-lighter dropped down a drain, and the astounding exploding-carousel finale. If the movie has one let-down it's in the slightly stilted heroic performances by Granger and Roman. Walker on the other hand gives the performance of a lifetime as one of Hitchcock's classic Oedipal psychos; effeminately pawing Guy one minute and hard-as-nails strangler the next. The film has a beautiful string score by Dimitri Tiomkin and is very handsomely shot (Hitch's first of twelve films with the great Robert Burks). A classic film of a classic crime novel.</fullReview>
	</review>
