		<date>2007-05-30</date>
		<username>naillon-2</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Another winner from Hitchcock</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hitchcock struck gold again with this dark tale of two strangers - one a famous tennis player, the other a layabout - who meet and swap stories of their lives. Bruno Antony (Robert Walker) is envious of the attractive, popular Guy Haines (Farley Granger), and being abreast of the latest news, Bruno knows that Guy is separated from his wife, but is finding it harder than he expected to divorce her.This is where things get sticky. Bruno mentions that the two of them could trade murders. Guy could marry Bruno's father, whom Bruno hates; Bruno, for his part, could murder Guy's wife. The perfect murder - "Criss-cross," as Bruno puts it.Guy laughs off Bruno's suggestion, but not long afterwards, he is informed by the police that his estranged wife, Miriam, has been found strangled. And Guy, who had a loud and even physical fight with her not long before she died, is the suspect. Not only that, but Bruno keeps appearing... and appearing... and appearing... impatient for Guy to hold up his end of the "bargain".Robert Walker, who would die in a couple of years, gives the best performance of his career as the charming, soft-spoken, psychopathic murderer, easily balancing out Granger's almost-too-good Guy Haines. We don't often see beneath the attractive exterior, but when we do, it's a dark and twisted vision. Bruno is even a literal stain on the landscape, such as in the scene where he is watching Guy from a flight of white steps; he is the only dark object in the frame. In another scene, during a tennis match, the heads of the spectators turn back and forth with the movement of the ball - all except for Bruno, who is watching Guy with a little smile on his face.Then, of course, there is the unbeatable climax, on board a merry-go-round that is spinning out of control. Pure genius. A must-see.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Strangers meet - one is very sorry</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What a wonderful, tense, exciting work Hitchcock's "Strangers on a Train" is! It is also a great testament to the talent of Robert Walker, who came to a sad end the very year this film was released. His Bruno Anthony is brilliant - demented, angry, fussy, charming when necessary, and scary as hell.But there are other wonderful performances in this film - there isn't much point in me telling the story, as many reviewers on this board have done so already. Patricia Hitchcock gives a delightful performance as Anne's sister (well portrayed by the lovely Ruth Roman) and makes us wish she had made more films; Farley Granger, in a role intended for William Holden, is boyishly handsome and likable as tennis player (soon to be politician) Guy Haines; and there are two Hitch favorites, Leo G. Carroll and Una O'Connor, in supporting roles.The finale is one of the most thrilling Hitch ever did, and even after repeated viewings has one squirming in one's seat.I viewed both the British and American versions. The homoeroticism so slightly suggested in the British version was toned down in the American initial meeting of Guy and Bruno by playing the dialogue faster, and for the American version, Hitchcock tacked on an ending suggestive of the beginning of the film, bringing it full circle. I admit that I actually prefer that to the British ending (which is the second-last scene in the American version). But I like the British scene where Guy and Bruno meet on the train better.Actually, it doesn't matter which one you see - it's a great movie.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2005-07-22</date>
		<username>silverscreen888</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Cold, Calculated and Fascinating Noir; Hitchcock's B/W Best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Strangers On a Train" is not Alfred Hitchcock's best film. But it is, however, an unusual psychological mystery, fine opportunity for the director to supply many artistic and memorable shots, and a quite suspenseful noir story-line in its own right. It's a clever adventure about a man who cannot go to the police, which endangers not only him but several others as well. The stars of this taut suspenseful tale are Farley Granger as Guy, tennis player and a man who wants very much to divorce his current wife so he can marry his new heartthrob, Ruth Roman. He wants to enter politics; so scandal is an impossibility for him. On a train, he meets a clever psychotic, Bruno Anthony, played by Robert Walker Jr.. Bruno has a father he needs to be rid of, in oder to gain his money and lose his restraints on his wild behavior. So he proposes a solution--the perfect murder, as an exchange...since they are 'strangers on a train' if Guy kills his father, Bruno will kill his wife. No motive; no possible connection; no danger from the police. Guy laughs--until his wife IS murdered and Bruno starts calling, demanding that Guy complete his part of the bargain. There are several great scenes thereafter--back-courter Guy trying to rush through a tennis match at Forest Hills so he can stop Bruno, since Bruno stalking an innocent relative played charmingly by bespectacled Patricia Hithere is a final confrontation on a ferris wheel. The film is a bit cold in feeling to me; but it moves like clockwork; and everything works, thanks to Walker's quirky portrayal of an emotionless killer who gets thrills only out of harming people, or considering how he will do it. The script went through many hands beginning as a Patricia Highsmith novel, an adaptation by Whitfield Cook at Hitchcock's direction, then work by Ben Hecht, Raymond Chandler and Czenzi Ormonde. It was Hitchcock's way to obtain and work on a story outline, have someone else write the first screenplay and then rework it to make it a better shooting script, with or without others' help. The fine B/W cinematography here was done by Robert Burks, art direction by Ted Haworth. George James Hopkins provided the fine set decorations and Leah Rhodes the very good costumes. Dimitri Tiomkoin did the original music. In the cast, Granger does well, Walker even better and Ruth Roman is a strong presence. Patricia Hitchcock created a genuine character. Others in the cast included Leo G. Carroll, pretty Kasey rogers as the luckless wife, Marion Lorne Mrs. Anthon, Jonathan Hale as Bruno's father, Howard St. John as the police captain, John Brown, Norma Varden and Robert Gist. In this film the most outstanding and memorable quality is the detail moments, such as Bruno losing down a grate something he desperately needs, Patricia Hitcock's glasses, Bruno trying his strength at a carnival concession, the moment at the tennis match when everyone in the crowd is watching the ball and turning their heads back and forth except Bruno who is staring at Guy, etc. Robert Walker was only a year away from suicide at this time; but he has enough control left here to do something remarkably well-thought-out. And Farley Granger was never better as a leading man than in this effort. This is an important work, I believe for Hitchcock; a developmental challenge taking him from his B/W period to his larger-scale color works in the U.S.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Christopher Hall (corridorhall)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Overrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The trouble with a film like ?STRANGERS ON A TRAIN,' is that from the very beginning I've become aggravated with a supposedly charming, yet devious character. The problem is, he isn't charming, but he is devious -- but not in a deliciously wicked kind of way. Watching him I feel disgusted -- I want the hero of the film to run away, or to report this character to the cops. But this doesn't happen -- for reasons I'll state soon enough.What happens, though, is that the tennis player (who as a celebrity, tries to play friendly with the common folk), makes the mistake of allowing the stranger (who is very obsessed with him), to become too friendly. But he isn't just obsessed by his status as a player, he's also got the athlete's personal life down pack. You see. He knows he's getting a divorce. And the lighter the athlete carries around, which contains him and his wife's first name initials -- the stranger knows what the initials stand for. And I wouldn't cancel out the possibility that he knows the size of his tennis shoes, and where he bought them.The tennis player finds himself in a bit of a pickle, when he sarcastically agrees to the stranger's proposition, that: the stranger kill the tennis player's wife, and that the tennis player kill the stranger's father -- `criss-cross,' the stranger calls it. But sarcasm is something the stranger did not pick-up. Oh no. For a night later, he strangles the Tennis player's wife to death at a carnival.Now, one may think, that once the tennis player receives news of this occurrence, he'll simply call the cops and quickly kick the stranger out of his life, for good. But there's a problem. The tennis player is an accessory to murder. The stranger asks, `Why would I kill a stranger?' Good point. It would seem plausible for a man to kill a woman who denies that they separate. So what must the tennis player do, in order to avoid imprisonment? Even I, who feel negative about the film, will not spoil.But speaking of irony. I often watched certain passages of this film, through the eyes of those who've loved it, and I see a film that isn't so bad after all. That's clearly the wrong way to watch any movie. Then I watch the film through my 18 year old eyes, and see mediocrity.I suppose, that for diehard Hitchcock fans, they see a personal work, which asks the question: `Would I ever kill someone?' and `how would I go about doing it?' This is obviously a question the filmmaker has posed one-time or the other, and I'd be lying if I said that I didn't.But what makes the movie Titanic for me, is that the screenwriter didn't use a more practical. universal situation that would bring up such a question, for every person who watched it -- or anyone like me.Movies, I know, are all about trips to: fantasy worlds, nightmares, and more practical locations. And there are movies that have gone the nightmarish, fantasy and pratical route, and I've loved them. But this movie. This one didn't quite move me enough.For quite a long time, I've been watching a few `classics,' and found that the only `classic' thing about them were their age. Either that, or I'd deny my state-of-mind at the moment, just because I'm not enjoying it as much as every other critic is. But my state of mind is just fine. This film, I'm sorry to say, is very overrated.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A Dark, Suspenseful Ride</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>One of Hitchcock's most entertaining films, "Strangers On A Train" has the combination of humor and suspense that fans of the director love. The wonderfully twisted character of Bruno, played with chilling brilliance by Robert Walker, is a classic villain. Farley Granger looks incredible and, while not ingenious, is perhaps well cast as Bruno's hapless victim. There are excellent performances from Ruth Roman, Leo G. Carroll, Pat Hitchcock and the hilarious Marion Lorne. And there are several quintessential Hitchcock moments: the argument in the record store listening booth and the cocktail party "strangulation" are two unforgettable examples. The film is beautifully shot, but Tiomkin's overly romantic score only works during quietly suspenseful scenes. The DVD issue is superb in every technical way: some scenes are so beautiful, they almost distract you from the story. Side two of the disc contains the British version (only slightly different from the US, but perhaps a must for fans). Extras include eight very well transferred ORIGINAL trailers from several Hitchcock films - they really are fun to see. This film didn't even make a recent Hitchcock "Ten Best" (but neither did "Rear Window"). If you like Hitchcock, do not miss this film.</fullReview>

		<username>planktonrules</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A truly exceptional film...with one glaring problem.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Don't get me wrong, I love and respect this film and it's among Alfred Hitchcock's best films. Yet, I still cannot see it as a perfect film because there is one glaring problem. Any NORMAL person would soon go to the police if they were in Farley Granger's situation...and not try to fix everything themselves or hope the problem would go away on its own. And, any suspense film that hinges on the idea off smart people temporarily acting dumb has got a knock against it--and surely doesn't deserve a 10. However, I do need to point out that aside from this, this is a great film. Robert Walker's performance is menacing and exciting to watch--and not typical of the usual film noir or suspense villain. He simply made the film. Also, aside from the brain lapse of the main character, the rest of the script is great--exciting, original and well worth seeing. A lovely film...but perhaps overrated within the Top 250 due to its plot difficulty.By the way, a GREAT double-feature would be to see this and "My Son, John". That's because these were Walker's last two movies and the actor died before completing "My Son, John". So, to salvage the film, a few clips from "Strangers on a Train" were inserted into the second film. Views of DC were reused as well as the final death scene that was clipped and inserted in a vehicle at the end of "My Son, John". Fascinating viewing.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Flawed and overrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I can't understand why Hitchcock's movies are so highly rated even when they have major logical loopholes. Look at this particular movie.* Bruno, who is quite smart otherwise, kills Miriam even when Guy clearly has rebuffed his suggestion in no uncertain terms that he won't be a part of the "criss-cross" scheme.* Why Bruno attracts so much attention at the carnival, hitting the hammer and chasing Miriam flirtatiously? * Why did the man who suspected Bruno of the murder didn't tell the police at the time of the murder? Didn't the police question everybody around there? * How did the boyfriends of Miriam leave her alone in the dark for so long on the island to allow Bruno to carry out his plan? From what we saw on the boat, they should be holding and grabbing her.* Going in the dark of the night to meet Bruno's father is also highly implausible. He could simply have called him and met him anywhere of his liking. And while we are at Bruno's father's house, why didn't Guy ring the bell instead of stealing inside with the help of the map? Also, what was the purpose of taking the gun with him? * What was the logic of Bruno's going to the island and planting the lighter, weeks after the murder? Surely the police would have scoured the island after the murder in search of clues, didn't they? Please don't get me wrong, I think the film is a nice thriller and provides good entertainment and has some great visuals. But a great film? Perfect in every way? A wonderful movie to be watched for a dozen times? Come on, boys.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Guy Haines is a tennis pro with a lover and a wife who has left him and is divorcing him. A chance meeting on a train with stranger Bruno Anthony sees the two of them get to talking and Bruno rambling about a vague scheme about strangers swapping murders ? for example Bruno killing Guy's spiteful wife and Guy killing Bruno's dominating father. That way neither man can be linked to either murder but both get what they want. Of course Guy leaves the train thinking no more of this babbling fool until, that is, he claims to have murdered Guy's wife ? a claim that turns out to be true. With Guy the police's number one suspect and Bruno pursuing him to meet "his end of the deal", Guy must work out a way out before it is too late.One of the best things about Christmas is possibly the fact that lots of older films get wheeled out in mini series of a director's or actor's work, with lots of the films over a short time period. This year, as with several years ago, Hitchcock was the man in question on one channel and I took the opportunity to revisit several of his films in that time. Having not seen Strangers for many years, I found that the majority of my memories of it actually came from the Billy Crystal film where he references it rather than the film itself; this meant that I didn't remember exactly what happens and could almost enjoy it as if it were the first viewing. As such the film is consistently gripping and enjoyable as things seem to come to a head quite quickly. At the point of Bruno's murder the film does stutter a little bit as it can't possibly keep up that momentum but even then it is still very good.Hitchcock has built the tension well within scenes and between characters and it is this that keeps the film moving along with the unknown resolution. The black and white looks crisp and very clear ? the use of shadows is good but done sparingly, with the majority of the film being very clear and perhaps more engaging for it. This was Walker's last film since he died a few months after making it and he is effectively unhinged here ? appearing coldly cruel and sane while also showing that he is very much off his head! For this reason he tends to steal his scenes easily from Granger who has more of a straight role at the start, although he does get markedly better as the film goes on and more is asked of him. Outside of these two only Elliott really has a large role and she does it well; meanwhile support is roundly good and features a small role for Carroll.Overall this is a great film. For sheer entertainment value I'm afraid that it isn't my favourite Hitchcock but it is still very good and is rightly still held in high esteem. The story is simple but is delivered at a good pace and with tension, the direction is good and the cast are roundly strong even if Walker tends to effortlessly steal his scenes. Overall a great film and one that is definitely making sure that you've seen.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A curiosity at best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not nearly one of his best films, not even in the top half of his body of work, SOAT is a curiosity at best. A great premise - two men who meet accidentally find out they have something in common, the wish to have someone "out of the way". Sadly, the greatness ends there. The remaining 90 minutes are contrived and frustratingly illogical. Sure, Walker's psycho is interesting, but that's about it. I've seen this movie several times, each time trying to see why other people like it as much as they do. Maybe I just don't get it, because I can't imagine why this movie elicits such high praise. There's no spark of intelligence or insight. There isn't even any amusing banter between any of the characters. Like "Shadow of a Doubt", this movie suffers from a serious lack of plot, bad acting, and careless storytelling.I am amazed at how badly the actors do with their roles: Granger looks like a statue of Gregory Peck on downers, and Roman has but two expressions. Still, I don't expect wonderful acting in a Hitchcock picture - that would just be a bonus. Instead, what I expect is tension, technique, and invention.There are some nice moments: the strangling sequence, the glass booths in the music store, underneath the merry-go-round. But there's no tension. Even the climactic sequence depends on a bizarre, pointless series of coincidences, each designed to rectify the fact that there is no real drama.And just to be picky, I'd like to complain about the, uh... how shall I say this... completely inadequate explanations for character motivations. Maybe in some alternate universe it would be believeable for these characters to behave as they do, but even in that universe this movie wouldn't be interesting enough for me to recommend it.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Not Hitchcock's best, but still a sign to future success.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is not Hitchcock's best film by a long shot, but it is still most have been a big deal back in 1951. There had never been a film quite like this prior to 1951. It is the kind of film that must have attracted and appealed to a wide international audience. This was a very exciting, youthful, and big production film that came into the fore during the 60s. It must have attracted a lot of attention at the time.Robert Walker plays a very disturbed, yet charming man in some ways. His character is extremely intelligent and extremely disturbed. He is the kind of man who people would trust instinctively until they got to know him a little bit more. Walker stalks a tennis pro (Farley Granger) and in a conversation on the train proposes that he murders his unfaithful wife and that his job will be to kill his hated father.Not surprisingly, Granger's character is blackmailed into "the proposal" after Walker's character decides to actually murder his unfaithful wife. Both characters are well developed. Granger's is a kind and manipulated man with a promising future. Walker's character is literally a "walking time bomb". He is very charming on the surface, but quickly alienates people with his "crazy talk".This movie is exciting. However, there are a few things that significantly detract from the film. First of all, Granger's character is too nice. Yes, he does say that he wants to kill his wife and even has a domestic dispute with her in public. However, he is a very nice guy. It would have worked better if he was less of the nice guy. Instead of being the victim of a psychopath's manipulation, he could have been better characterized as someone who was pretty normal and decent on the surface, but rather amoral in reality. That would have made him make a tacit agreement and actually fulfill his part in the bargain. This would have made the story far more entertaining and morally complex.Instead, Hitchcock settled for the typical "good guy" versus "bad guy". This made the movie seem a bit hallow and overproduced. The ending of the movie was a bit too contrived. First of all, why would the police shoot bullets into a crowded carousel with children on it? This killed the attendant on the carousel and caused the carousel to rocket out of control. The ending was a bit too much of a sellout, where the "good guy" lives happily ever after. Still, it must have been a big deal in 1951. I don't think that prior to this time there was such a big production movie capable of appealing to such a mass, younger audience.</fullReview>

		<username>Coventry</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A Stranger is a Psychopath you haven't met....</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Admittedly I still have a long way to go before I can call myself a Hitchcock-connoisseur (more into rancid and exploitative cinema myself), but I can easily comprehend why this "Strangers on a Train" is often named as one of the director's finest achievements and as one of the greatest thrillers in cinema history all together. The basic "perfect murder" outline is simply terrific, the film features one of the most fascinating psychotic villainous characters ever (Bruno Anthony) and several of the classic sequences literally ooze with atmosphere and suspense like you've rarely seen it before. On the train to his hometown of Metcalf, the famous tennis player Guy Haines makes acquaintance with the slightly obtrusive traveler Bruno Anthony. Anthony is a clearly disturbed but intelligent and eloquent individual, since he seems to know a lot about Haines' private life and almost promptly elaborates his plan to commit the perfect murder. He claims that if two complete strangers could "swap" murders, both crimes would appear motiveless and the main suspect would have a waterproof alibi, so the authorities will never be able to solve them. Under the impression that he convinced Haines of his sardonic scheme, Bruno commits his murder (Haines' greedy ex-wife who refuses to divorce him) and afterwards confronts the tennis star with his part of the imaginary bargain. When he refuses, Bruno Anthony infiltrates into his personal life and manically enlarges the web of guilt surrounding Guy Haines by making him out as the culprit towards his friends and relatives.This is another example of a film that almost entirely relies on the villainous character of the story. Bruno Anthony is a truly compelling and deeply disturbing character, and I caught myself cheering for him instead of for the wooden character of Guy Haines. Now, I often root for the bad guys in films, but here the situation was still somewhat different. Bruno Anthony's allegedly malignant ideas, as well as the surefooted and emotionless executions of them, are so vastly ingenious and well-structured that you simply have to respect and admire him, even though he's probably more dangerous than any mad-raving psychopath running loose on the streets. Remarkably enough, in all his evil glory, Bruno nevertheless also remains an ordinary human being who occasionally miscalculates or makes mistakes (like dropping a lighter into a drain), which then again results in a handful of extremely powerful suspense and race-against-time sequences. Robert Walker's performance as Bruno Anthony reaches damn near to absolute perfection, as he's one of the most chilling and stone-cold actors I ever watched on a screen. When I looked up his biography and repertoire on this website, I instantly felt sad discovering that Walker passed away in the same year as this film's release already. Sad, because this role could have been the start of a very promising further career.Hitchcock's "Strangers on a Train" is a full-blooded suspense and action thriller, but it nevertheless contains a handful of artistic highlights that become particularly unforgettable since they depict moments of pure agony and horror. I'm obviously talking about the despicable murder ? shown through the reflection of the victim's fallen glasses ? but also about some more notable Point-of-View shots. Other vastly memorable highlights include the conversation between strangers at the beginning (of course), the exhilarating and adrenalin-pumping Carrousel climax and ? my personal favorite scene - Bruno Anthony explaining to two elderly ladies how murderous lust is a natural sentiment and how bare hands are the ideal weapons. It's a fantastic film, as I think I stated clear already, but in all honesty it isn't entirely without flaws or minor defaults, neither. Some quintessential story lines seem a bit underdeveloped, like for example the hostile relationship between Bruno and his father (the person he would like to see getting killed) and his spontaneous interest towards Barbara (Guy Haines' future sister in law). With the exception of Robert Walker, the only other perplexing performance is given by Kasey Rogers as Miriam, Haines' deviant ex-wife. The rest of the cast seems somewhat wooden, although this might just be one of the master of suspense's tricks to illustrate their ignorance and powerlessness.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Hitchcock was truly a great director, as is shown in such masterpieces as "Vertigo," "North by Northwest," and "The Birds." Typically, "Strangers on a Train" is also listed among his finest achievements; people will tell you that it's much deeper than a normal thriller because it is not merely a wrongfully accused man who seeks to prove his innocence, because the wrong man knows that he is, in fact, not innocent. If this were the case, "Strangers on a Train" would truly be a great film, but sadly, Farley Granger's performance doesn't allow it.Textually, the film is built around the principle of a doppelganger; two men meet each other on a train, each of whom envy each other. The crazy man envies the star athlete who has found true love and security in the world with a bright future in politics, and the star athlete envies the crazy man who could murder without troubling his conscience because he has an unfaithful wife who is inconvenient to his romance with a senator's daughter. Thus, when the crazy man murders the star athlete's wife due to a mix-up, the star athlete is not completely innocent, because of his desire for her murder.Unfortunately, this only exists on a textual level because Farley Granger plays his star athlete like any wrongfully accused man seeking to prove his innocence. We don't believe him when he says that he could just strangle his wife on the phone; he is too nice to be blamed. The films central struggle should not be the athlete's attempt to prove his innocence, but his attempt to come to terms with his conscience, knowing that, even though he did not murder her himself, it was his murder.I much prefer Orson Welles' "The Trial" to this film. It does not contain a doppelganger, but it is about a man who is arrested without knowing what crime he is charged with, but becomes convinced of his own guilt in the process of trying to prove his innocence. Anthony Perkins' performance in that film is much greater at showing the confused sense of guilt that is central to "The Trial," and that should have been central to "Strangers on a Train."That said, it is quite an enjoyable film, and I would recommend seeing it in spite of its shortcomings.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hitchcock keeps Farley Granger from ruining his movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Downright bad performances from everyone except Robert Walker and Marion Lorne (as a suave psychopath and his creepy, deranged mother) make it impossible to care, for instance, about the relationship between supposed romantic leads Farley Granger and Ruth Roman. Said relationship appears to have been inserted in a very artificial manner, probably because a) it's always been one of Hollywood's (nonsensical) rules that every movie has to have a love story, and b) existence of said love story would take the focus off of the much more interesting (and in those days unacceptable) one going on between Granger and Walker. The script is surprisingly lame at times, considering it was co-written by Raymond Chandler.Director Alfred Hitchcock overcame some of these hurdles by bringing daringly close to the fore the homoerotic link between the title strangers, which adds heft to the film. It helps that Walker, who clearly got into his role, isn't afraid of a little suggestiveness. All scenes involving his character are fascinating. The movie suffers from an awkward editing job, but Hitchcock orchestrates many stylish and potent scenes, such as the murder of Granger's ex, the 'pretend' strangling at the party, and the terrifying carousel climax.</fullReview>

		<username>codefool</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Interesting premise that doesn't deliver</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>***SPOILERS*** ***SPOILERS*** I belive Hitchcock to be one of the great cinematic geniuses of our time. "Strangers on a Train" is no small tribute to his ability to generate and maintain suspense and morbid danger despite a mediocre story.The premise is interesting enough - two men meet on a train and one suggests that they "switch" murders. That is, each one wants someone dead. So man A kills man B's victim, giving man B the opportunity to arrange a solid alibi. Further, since man A has no connection what-so-ever with man B's victim, he is never considered a suspect, or even comes into purview of the investigation. The perfect murder? Could be.The problem is that you don't have two men who want to murder - you just have one. Man A kills man B's victim without man B wanting it to happen. Then you have this mish-mash of "plot" where man A is trying to coerce man B into killing his victim.While the writers of this mess certainly tried to cover all their bases, it just doesn't gell. Man A shows up to man B and says "I've done your killing for you - now you have to kill mine." Man B wants to go to the police - but man A gives the thinnest of arguments that man B would be considered an accomplice to murder. Sorry, even in 1951 that just doesn't work. Also, even though man B's victim is his wife, he shows absolutely no remorse about her grisly death.Having said that, Hitchcock's work is excellent. Like a great paint job on an old car, the film is nice to look at for cinematic qualities, and the camera work and dialog are great. However, if you want a story that grabs you and appeals to you intellectually, then see "Dial M for Murder" instead.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Never has a movie been so simultaneously brilliant and dumb</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERSStrangers on a Train is generally regarded as one of Hitchcock's masterpieces. It shouldn't be. Well, maybe it should. This film has such a dual nature it's almost maddening. Hitchcock's direction was hardly ever this tight and perfect - it's a huge credit to him that he can make scenes that ought to seem idiotic, and do, when you stop to think about them, as suspenseful as Hell. The reason for this is that he does not let you stop to think about them, but chugs forward like a freight train. Take even the merry-go-round finale - no matter how desperately Hitch tries to play the scene for comedy, it ends up seeming utterly ridiculous. But the direction is so brilliant, it becomes exciting. Eventually, the almost insulting bits of stupidity pile up too high and I, at least (and I do know that many others are with me on this), end up angry. Still, I cannot in any way deny the brilliance of Hitch's skill. I am forced to hand this one an 8/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"We planned it together, criss-cross."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The film has a highly intriguing premise, and for about half the film it works. However by the time it should really be getting interesting, elements slip in that defy credibility and work against any semblance of achieving a satisfying conclusion. Probably the most imposing of these is the resemblance of Anne Morton's (Ruth Roman) sister (portrayed by Hitchcock's daughter Patricia) to murder victim Miriam. It comes in handy for putting Bruno (Robert Walker) into a trance at the party as he recreates the strangling, but gee!, why didn't anyone figure out he was a nut case right then and there?Anne Morton attempts to convince Mrs. Anthony that her son might be a murderer - did she really think she could make an impression with that argument? Same story when Bruno tells Anne he's going to place the cigarette lighter back at the scene of the crime. The criminal mastermind didn't really have to signal his intention, did he? And why was it so important to return it at night? The best though was when Bruno asked the amusement park guy "What time does it get dark around here?" It reminded me of a 'Candid Camera' situation where they asked people on the street 'What time is it?' and someone answered, 'I don't know, I'm not from around here.' It's too bad Bruno didn't follow up with the tactic of planting a seed of doubt with Anne, by putting the finger on her boyfriend Guy (Robert Walker) as the killer. Had the film explored that avenue, I believe there could have been a more suspenseful resolution. Instead, we have an innocent bystander killed by the cops (the merry go round operator), and no one really seems to notice. It should be so easy in real life, a cigarette lighter falls from a dead man's hand and it lets the suspected murderer off the hook, no questions asked, just like that, amen.At least Hitch himself isn't hard to spot in this one; he's getting on the train when Haines gets off. As for Robert Walker, I thought he had the perfect "Twilight Zone" face, it's too bad he passed away a year after the film came out.I'd like to be more positive about "Strangers On A Train", but the story didn't really do it for me. Even as entertainment, I tend to view what's happening in a film with a critical eye to see if it passes a credibility test. This one didn't, and it's too bad, as it could have been a much more effective tune up for Hitchcock's later master work - "Psycho".</fullReview>

		<username>Michael McGonigle (mmcgonigle@philamuseum.org)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>This Is One Of Hitchcock's Least Innocent Films, Truly Nasty.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You could write a college essay on the criss-crossing imagery in Alfred Hitchcock's 1951 film Strangers On A Train. The film begins by following two sets of shoes as they walk through a train station. One is an elegant pair of spats and the other is a cheaper pair of plain dark shoes. Then we see the train tracks as they cross through a confluence of switches and connections. A moment later in the club car these two pairs of shoes meet when one pair crosses his legs and their toes tap. Even the initial connection between "good" Guy (Farley Granger) and "evil" Bruno (Robert Walker) is made by a criss-cross.This wholly innocent opening leads into one of Hitchcock's least innocent films. With it's startling violence, realistic marital discord, irreverent black humor and not so latent homo-eroticism, Strangers On A Train must have been shocking to people back in the fifties because it can still shock today.The basic premise is that Bruno wants his father dead. Accidentally meeting Guy on the train gives him a chance to expound on one of his "theories". Knowing from newspaper accounts that tennis pro Guy is having problems with his wife Miriam who will not give him a divorce, he proposes that they swap murders, Bruno will kill the wife, then Guy kills the father. By swapping the murders they both get what they want and more importantly, they will both get away with it. Guy condescendingly agrees realizing he's dealing with a nut; but Bruno is deadly serious.So, Bruno follows Miriam one night to an amusement park and cruises her to a lovers island. Then he grabs her viscously by the throat and begins to strangle hard; her agonies visible in the reflection from her glasses that have fallen to the ground. It's one of Hitchcock's most sexy/brutal murders. Proud of what he's done (I mean Bruno, not Hitchcock; or do I?), Bruno now wants Guy to fulfill his end of the bargain.There are other memorable scenes; the tennis match where everybody's head is turning right and left following the match while Bruno never takes his eyes off Guy. The elegant party scene where Bruno demonstrates his strangling technique to near disaster and the finale aboard the wild merry-go-round.The real revelation in Strangers On A Train is Robert Walker as dear, demented Bruno Anthony. Like many a Hitchcock villain, he's intelligent, charming and completely psychopathic. However, in this film, Hitchcock ups the ante; there is no mistaking Bruno's attraction to the lithe, young, tennis pro. As he says plaintively at one point, "But Guy. . . I like you."Still, despite the seemingly great premise, the guys on the TV show Law &amp; Order would have solved this case in an hour. If Miriam is murdered, Guy is automatically a suspect. There are many cases where the real murderer has an alibi. That's because he's hired someone else to do the dirty work. The police are not stupid. Trust me, don't think too hard and you will enjoy the thrills of Strangers On A Train and good luck with the college essay.</fullReview>
	</review>

In the first place were it to work, it would be awkward and difficult to maintain no proof that the 2 ever met (as evidenced in the movie).In short; Hitchcock may be entertaining to mystery lovers, but he is lamentable to crime lovers, because here the whole story appears very implausible and slapped together. His only range for brilliance consists of an entire movie centered around setting of a man who can do nothing but look out of his apartment window. 		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>One of Hitchcock's best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Roger Ebert has this movie on his list of all-time greatest movies, but to this day I do not understand why. There is no doubt that Hitchcock was a master of his craft, but directors have highs and lows just as athletes do, and I am not all together certain that Hitchcock was at the top of his game in this movie.There are memorable moments such as the turning heads at a tennis match, when the protagonist Guy spots his stalker as only the one focused on him. Bruno seeing his reflection off the glasses of his victim is a classic scene. And the merry-go-round gone out of control is a dazzling array of colors that builds up real suspense toward the final resolution. Yet despite the technical brilliance of a handful of scenes, the problem that I find with the movie is the weakness of the plot and the relative mildness of the suspense.At times, Hitchcock seems to be straining the film in order make it suspenseful. During one point in the movie, he uses interweaving shots of Guy playing desperately at his tennis match and the antagonist Bruno struggling to pick up a lighter he dropped into a sewer drain. Hitchcock uses music to create suspense with the shots, but it's as awkward as a Wagner opera. The suspense simply isn't there, and the music is being forced to get the job done.It isn't a bad movie by any means, but I consider it one of Hitchcock's weaker attempts because his presence in the movie is too obvious. Whenever the flow of the movie begins to slow, Hitchcock is there to throw something extra into the mix. However, it isn't enough. There are very few surprises in the movie, and every attempt at a twist seems to fall just short of it's goal. I recommend the movie if only to see Robert Walker's disturbing portrayal of Bruno, whose creepiness rivals even Hannibal Lecter. However, for the movie as a whole, I find it disappointing in spite of all the great reviews.</fullReview>

		<username>blacksheep99</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Hitchcock's themes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is certainly gay subtext in Strangers, and Hitch seems to have believed that he was some sort of amateur psychologist, who had discovered that criminal psychopathology is somehow related to gender bending types like Norman Bates in Psycho, who dresses in his mother's clothes and has an odd relationship with her, to say the least. Here too, we see two of Hitchcock's three themes (as defined by Rohmer and Chabrol in their book on Hitchcock): (1) the influence of Catholicism, (2) the theme of shared guilt, and (3) homophobia and misogyny. The latter two are certainly present here, and there is no doubt in my mind that Hitch meant to imply that Bruno was a homosexual who, like all homosexuals in Hitch's mind, had an overly controlling mother and an aversion to his father. Well, Hitch, in his role as amateur psychologist, certainly attributes homosexuality to nurture, not nature. Hitch was a great master of film form, but a lousy psychologist.. He should have stuck to film-making and left the analysis to the experts.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Good but not Great</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just like many Hitchcock films, this one seems a bit overrated. The acting overall is good - except for Fontaine, who appears forced and unnatural. The storyline is simple and easy to follow. It would be hard to believe they would come back several nights later in case they missed something as obvious as a cigarette lighter with someones initials laying in the grass.Just as many viewers have already said, the sheriff killing the innocent merry-go-around employee with no-one else reacting is too far-fetched.Upon entering Bruno's father's home, Haines comes across the growling guard dog but magically gets the dog to trust him.Haines plays tennis while Bruno accidentally drops the lighter in the drainage. 		<username>writers_reign</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Off The Rails</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hitchcock may be, as I seem to be saying over and over - vastly overrated but no one could say the same of Ray Chandler who co-authored this adaptation of Patricia Highsmith's first successful novel. Highsmith created a territory of her own as distinctive in its was as that of Faulkner or Graham Greene and even Hitch couldn't damage it seriously. Here the premise is ingenious; a psychopath, encountering a normal man on a train proposes that they 'exchange' murders; being total strangers they would, if the proposition was carried to a logical conclusion, be murdering total strangers for which they had no motive. The twist is, of course, that the psycho, Bruno Anthony (Robert Walker) is kidding on the level and definitely wants to see his father murdered whilst Guy Haines (Farley Granger) who does have a wife he is attempting to divorce, merely humours Bruno. From here things spiral out of control, Bruno does, in fact, kill Guy's wife and expects no less in return. For a Hitchcock movie this isn't bad and certainly watchable.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2000-07-18</date>
		<username>Spleen</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>So many people get it so wrong</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A long way from being one of Hitchcock's better films. Someone here said that it was his first "truly" American film (which means ...?); in fact it's ALMOST a first of another kind. It's the second of his films (the first was "Notorious") about which a great deal of rubbish is routinely written. Here's a sample, from Donald Spoto's preposterous "The Art of Alfred Hitchcock":"But the final image suggests things may not turn out so well. ... A passenger across the aisle leans forward and repeats Bruno's opening line in the film: 'Excuse me, but aren't you Guy Haines?' Guy and Ann look at each other and without replying quickly move away from this new stranger on a train. But the questioner is a minister, and this carries an omen beneath the humour. This couple should be moving toward the minister, not away from him, willing to reply to crucial questions of identity and ... [blah, blah, blah] ...The life of order and quiet dignity to which he aspires is in grave doubt indeed."Can you believe it? A rather ordinary final joke has been turned into something positively tedious. THAT'S what we get when the neo-gobbledegook symbol-hunters set to work on Hitchcock: tedium beyond measure. When we hear that Guy's life is a straight line, and Bruno's is a circle, but that Bruno "converts" Guy by inducing him to step onto a fairground merry-go-round ... well, it's hard not to yawn. When we hear that the film has two of everything (because there are two protagonists - get it?), we say, "So what?"; and then, a minute later: "Hang on - no it doesn't." Symbols should, like the musical symbols in Wagner's Ring, pulse with life. The symbols that one discovers in Hitchcock, if one can be said to discover anything as a result of this kind of Freudian dream analysis, are such dull and desiccated things they actually DIMINISH the films.Then there are those who claim that Bruno is Guy's dark side, that Guy is in some sense just as guilty as Bruno for wishing his wife dead, that Guy is really capable of the murder Bruno in fact commits, etc. That last claim is particularly absurd, or vacuous, depending on how you take it. Guy doesn't have a murderous bone in his body. It's true he wants his wife "out of the way"; assuming he can think reasonably clearly, this means he wants her dead; but there's no indication that he wants her murdered, still less that he is capable of murdering her himself. Bruno is NOT Guy's dark side. Bruno derives whatever menace he has from being an EXTERNAL force latching itself on to Guy, despite the fact that they have nothing in common, and nothing to do with one another.Film studies types fall over one another in their haste to morally condemn Guy, but what, exactly, is his crime? So far as I can see he commits only one: he never tells the truth to the police. But he has been forced into a false position by the director. Hitchcock once said, about his films in general, "They don't go to the police because it's dull"; well, in this case, Guy doesn't go to the police because if he did the film would be over. For almost all of the film all we are waiting for is for the police to discover the truth. GUY isn't weak - the plot is. The "strangers swapping murders" idea might be a good one, but I'm not sure a good story can be made of it; if one can be, this isn't it. After the murder things just stall. It's worth noting that the film contains little suspense, and what suspense there is (the tennis match, the lighter-down-the-drain sequence) feels contrived.I feel I've been too harsh. The weak plot isn't so weak as to be no fun at all: Bruno is a fine villain and Hitchcock gets good mileage out of him. And there's some superb photography. The strangling scenes are brashly creative without feeling at all overdone. Overall: nice, but unsatisfying.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hitchcock's Very Best Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Strangers On A Train" is the best of all Alfred Hitchcock's output.This movie is a major improvement over the book from which it was taken.Usually, it is the other way around, but in this case, the movie is a major improvement over the original book.I had seen this wonderful movie at least a dozen times, before I managed to find a copy of the book it was taken from....the book has the same title and was written by Patricia Highsmith.I scoured the used bookstores for years, before I finally found a copy, and because the movie was SO good, I could not wait to begin reading the story in its original version.I was never so disappointed!Not because the book is unreadable...but because Hitchcock made such vast improvements over the book that the book simply does not come close to measuring up to the movie version.That said, let me now comment on Robert Walker's amazing performance as Bruno Antony.This was Robert Walker's last completed performance...he died while shooting his final film, "My Son John," in August, 1951.This role as Bruno was the performance of his career!Perfect in every way.The movie has been around now for nearly half a century. I see it every time it is shown on television, and I also watch the tape I have of it occasionally.Robert Walker's performance only seems to improve with each new viewing.I can not recommend this movie highly enough.If Hitchcock and Robert Walker can read me, up there in heaven, let me congratulate them both on an absolutely superlative job!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>One of Hitchcock's best.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>WARNING: there may be spoilers in this commentary.One of Hitchcock's best films. The plot is outstanding, even the dialogues are very memorable. Anthony Perkins, Vera Miles, John Gavin and Janet Leigh star in this all time thriller/horror classic. That frightning old house and the motel make the perfect place to set such a mysterious plot where the truth seems to be the only thing missing in this film. The shower scene is really thrilling, also the falling-from-the-stairs sequence, and believe it or not but "Mother's" corpse is really the long awaited CLIMAX of the film (after the shower). This one is deffinately a psychological film. Alfred Hitchcock's best. Analyze Marion's paranoic behavior and Norman's quote "We all get into our private traps but sometimes we can never get out". And when she finally clears up her mind and regrets her act, she just has no way out. Bernard Herrmann's musical score couldn't be better (considering the type of film). Academy Award winner Martin Balsam also appears on this film. Too bad that this film had three sequels, and a remake (the worst). This film was at the Academy Awards of 1960. Among its many nominations are, Best Director (Alfred Hitchcock), best supporting actress (Janet Leigh), and Best cinematography in Black and White.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Worst film ever.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've finally given up on Hitchcock, he is to me, the most boring filmmaker I've ever come across. Even watching documentaries about him make me doze off. I recently watched strangers on a train because my house mate insisted it was a 'classic'. His 'classics' are usually borefests, IE: rear window, notorious, sabotage etc. I don't understand the hype behind this man, his films, and i mean all of them are dull, uninspired, bland and just downright boring. In fact i have to struggle to keep my eyes open, even at midday. As a student I've never got so much rest thanks to Alfred. His films are a cure for insomnia. Doctors should have a library full of his collection ready to give out to insomniacs. Along with 2001 space Odyssey and pretty much everything David lynch has made, strangers on a train ranks up there with the worst films ever made in my opinion. And this is the thing, to curse Hitchcock seems to be as bad as blaspheme. When you don't conform to the norm that he is great, everybody's jaw hits the floor (especially film students), and everybody has a pre-planned answer to why he's so amazing. They rabble on about his themes, motifs, range, narrative blah blah blah, but it doesn't make an ounce of difference if his films are unwatchable. Frankly, i don't see what range he has, all of his films are crime stories, and his fear of being wrongly accused borders on obsession. He pounds out film after film about that same crap. For crime films, id turn to Tarantino. Another common statement i hear is that Hitchcock's films inspired future directors and their films so we should all bow down and be grateful to the master. Personally i don't see what todays films have in common with his, they're superior in every-way. I hear that the matrix has nothing on Rear window, what? Are you mad? Matrix as cart-man likes to say, 'blew my warped little mind', Rear window on the other hand, just plain bored my mind. I'm allergic to black &amp; white films. Anything, well before the 1970's is a chore to watch except cuckoos nest. How can you compare the bright visuals of 21st century technology, the CGI, crisp sound and visuals to the humdrum bland films of the 30's. And so my film student friends tell me that its story that matters, plot elements, script, dialogue, structure etc. Yes but its hard to get to that when what you see up there is unappealingly boring. I've read threads with people complaining about a possible remake of this film, great i say, stick brad Pitt and ed Norton in there, Tarantino at the helm, don't think id be bored then me thinks. David Lynch is weird, so are his films, they're incomprehensible and incoherent. But he knows hes weird, he's kind of in his own bubble fleshing out his own odd perception of the world, converts it to film and sticks it in the cinema in the hope someone will watch it. Hes an acquired taste. But Hitchcock is mainstream, in nearly every critics top 5 directors list, named the master of suspense. With very bold statements like that i was expecting a joyride from his films. All of them rated above 8.0 on IMDb. This in my mind, is a whole collection of usual suspects/pulp fiction/fight club/matrix etc all over again. And from the same director, boy what a talent. Not. Strangers on a train didn't register a blip in my pulse. It was a complete non-event. There was not one moment i felt an ounce of terror or suspense. It was pointless, a waste of an hour and a half. And i feel the same about all his films. How the hell can this guy claim to be the master of suspense. I certainly don't believe everyone out there does either. Sure there are loads of people who genuinely adore him and his films, but i don't think I'm alone. Converesely, Einstein is a genius, i know this but i also know why. So do a lot of people. But there are many, many people who know about e=mc2, but don't know what it means, don't know what Einstein achieved and so on. Yet these people will all claim that Einstein is a genius. Why? not because they know it for themselves, but because its what the masses say so it must be true. Now I'm not saying he wasn't a genius, I'm merely illustrating the herd mentality. And I'm positive that many people out there hate Hitchcock's films but just don't admit it because of the mass consensus that hes a great, this is especially true of film students who feel they're doing something wrong if they cant see how amazing Mr Hitchcock is. I've started watching this film 7 times, and fell asleep every single time. I only came back to it to find out what happens at the end. On the last occasion, with a lot of coffee in my system, i managed to sit through it. When it ended i had a different reaction than usual. Usually i shrug my shoulders, and walk off cursing how crap the film was. Then within 20 minutes its forgotten. The film is out of my mind and completely forgotten, cant say that about the matrix. But this time i was disgraced at how pathetic an ending it was. Complete anti-climax. I've seen articles about how Hitchcock 'apparently' stirs up suspense in his films. I read the article, i understand the article, i watch the screen and me no see the suspense. I did buy a copy of this film, and it will forever be at my bedside table, for those nights that i just cant get to sleep.</fullReview>
